A story that con
cerns a certain
major in this
State, who is one
of the leading
spirits in Repub
lican councils,
runs something
One afternoon the aforesaid major came
up from the lower country, accompanied
by his wife. It happened that there was
going to be a prize fight by two well
known fistic celebrities on that night, and
although he would dearly have loved to
witness it he had yielded to the earnest
solicitations of his better half, who did
not believe "in those degrading exhibi
tions." and had promised her not to go.
but to take her to the theater instead. Just
as they were entering the Palace they
met a friend, who stopped and said, "Ah,
major, come up to attend the prize fight,
eh?" He did not notice the darkening
brow of the lady nor the appealing wink
of her husband, but went on. "You bet
you wouldn't miss one of those contests."
The dense friend only quit his badlnag*
when the madame said cur*'y, "Sir, the
major never attends those brutal af
fairs.
LOST FAITH
IN
NEWSPAPERS.
The next morning: the major was look
ing over the newspaper, mainly to see the
result of the previous night's contest,
when, his eye was attracted by a picture
which filk-d a quarter of a page, with the
caption, "Prominent people at the fight,"
and heading the procession in unmistaka
ble caricature was the major himself and
under it his name in large, bold type. He
experienced quite a shock on seeing it,
and going to his wife said, "My dear, did we
not go to the theater last nignt?" She re
plied, "We certainly did, and enjoyed it."
"Then," said the major, "look at this and
see how these villainous newspapers can
prevaricate about things." And the lady
saw the picture of her husband and the
notice of his attendance at the fight.
"Now," said the major, "you see these
newspapers are. not to be depended on,"
and his wife said, "I will never believe
one of them again."

Frank Dinsmore, one of the old-time
pioneers of Alaska, who has been lying
critically ill for several months at the
Commercial Hotel in this city, was able
to leave yesterday for Alma, Cal. There
are favorable developments In his case,
and it is now considered possible that he
will live for some years. The nature of
his malady has not been understood by
the doctors. Dinsmore is one of the most
widely known of the Yukon traders, hav
ing entered Central Alaska over twenty
years ago. When brought down here by
friends early in the summer it was not
thought possible that he could survive
many days.

HUMOR A PRECIOUS GIFT.
I regard a sense of humor as one of the
most precious gifts that can be vouch
safed to a human being. He is not neces
sarily a better man for having it, but he is
a happier one. It renders him indifferent
to good or bad fortune. It enables him to
ehioy his own discomfiture. Blessed with
this sense, he is never unduly elated or
cast down. No one can ruffle his temper.
No abuse disturbs his equanimity. Bores
do not bore him. Humbugs do not hum
bug him. Solemn airs do not impose on
him. Sentimental gush does not influence
him. The follies of the moment have no
hold on him. Titles and decorations are
but childish baubles in his .eyes. Preju
cice does not warp his judgment. He is
n.-ver in conceit or out of conceit with
himself. He abhors all dogmatism. The
world is a stage on which actors strut and
fret for his edification and amusement,
and he pursues the even current of his
way, invulnerable, doing what is right
and proper according to his lights, but
utterly indifferent whether what he does
finds approval or disapproval from others.
If Hamlet had had any sense of humor
he would not have been a nuisance to
himself and to all surrounding him.âLon
don Truth.
SENSIBLE SELF-CONFIDENCE.
One of the important results of the war
is the development of a rational self
confidence among the American people.
When President McKinley delivered the
ultimatum of Congress to Spain a vast
number of business men throughout the
United States regarded the future with
gloomy forebodings. Spain's paper navy
loomed large in their eyes. The supposed
possibility of the bombardment of sea
port towns created something like a panic
in Boston and other New England cities.
Predictions that business would be pros
trated, that industry would be struck
with paralysis and that the nation 8 cred
it would be shaken to its foundations,
were current in that portion of the press
which* vehemently opposed the ejectment
of Spain from Cuba.
We have been at war for two months,
and the utterances of the alarmists who
foretold widespread financial disturbance
and popular distress have already been
forgotten. The nation is buying and sell
ing, cultivating the soil, manufacturing
goods for domestic consumption and for
eign use and engaging in all its usual
forms of peaceful activity without inter
ruption. Bank clearings show a marked
increase in the general volume of busi
ness. The railroads are reaping larger
profits from the transportation of troops
and supplies. Thi- soundness of the coun
try's credit has been magnificently dem
onstrated, and it is recognized on all sides
that the Government can, if necessary,
dispose of the entire $500,000,000 in bonds
and certificates authorized by Congress
without going abroad to ask for a single
dollar.
These are lessons that deserve to be
heeded. They do not prove that war is a
desirable thing, but they do demonstrate
the latent strength of this republic and
the stability of our financial system,
faulty though it may be in some particu
lars. Nations, like men, need faith in
themselves. The events of the last sixty
days have brought with them many com
pensations.âPhiladelphia Bulletin.

KINGDOMâ S. M., City. The reason
that Great Britain is not called a "queen
dom" is because the term is not recog
nized. There is no essential difference
between the titles of King and Emperor,
but in ancient times the latter ranked
above the former. The term Emperor is
of Roman origin, while King is of Teu
tonic origin.

RENOUNCING CITIZENSHIPâ A. B.
I H.. City. This correspondent says: "Ten
j years ago I became an American citizen
and voted at the election of 1&S8. I the i
returned to Australia, resided there about
three years, took some interest in the
elections there and voted there. I have
now returned to California for good. I
wish to know if I could have registered
for the coming elections?" 1.-nder the cir
cumstances stated the correspondent
could not have been registered, as since
his return be Is as much an alien as if
he had never been naturalized in the
United States. A citizen of the Unite*
States who leaves the United States for
a foreign country, takes up his res dence
there and takes part in the affairs or
that foreign country, such as voting at
elections, by his acts renounces his citi
zenship as a citizen of the I nited StaUs
and assumes the citizenship of the coun
try in which he has taken up his abort,'
and exercised the rights of an elector
Upon his return to this country, wishing
to be a citizen thereof, he must proceed
exactly as if he had never been here be
fore The rule is that a man cannot
be a citizen of two nations at the same
time, and he must elect which nation he
will be a citizen of. The moment he
makes that election he surrenders the
I citizenship of the nation he turned his
back upon.
GERMANY'S DAY DREAM.
Germany, it seems, cherishes the dream
of being still further aggrandized at the
expense of its European neighbors. About
the only way that it can justify itself in
set-king to bring about a realization of this
dream is to assume that it is best for
people of the same blood to be united.
When the German empire was formed at
the close of the Franco-Prussian War, not
all of the Germans were included by any
means. There are some 20,000,000 of Euro
pean Germans who are not citizens of
the German empire. There are 10,000,000
Germans in Austria, and Germany sees
in its mind's eye the day when Trieste
will be a German port and the empire will
have a Mediterranean outlet. Then there
are 3,000,0u0 Germans in the Baltic prov
inces of Russia, to Bay nothing of the Ger
mans in Holland, Belgium and Switzer
land. There seems to be a good many
obstacles in the way of gathering in all
of these Germans, but it is said to be the
actual, though not the avowed, policy of
Germany to overcome these difficulties.
Already a sentiment favorable to Ger
i man unity is being cultivated wherever
there are Germans. The scheme is worthy
of the devotion of German statesmanship.
\ The annexations contemplated would be
annexations of contiguous territory and
! homogeneous people. It would be a genu
ine accession of national greatness â moro
than all of the islands Germany might get
i could be. Germany would better grow as
â  much as it can in Europe before it gives
, Its attention to far-away colonization. In
] the meantime it would better keep on the
j good side of Great Britain and the United
States.â Cleveland Plain Dealer.
SPAIN'S FINANCIAL COLI^APSE.
It is estimated in Madrid that the war
is costing Spain 528,000,0(50 a month in
direct expenditure, not mentioning indi
rect cost.
Since April the Bank of Spain has ad
vanced $40,000,000 cm treasury bonds, tak
ing a mortgage on the revenues, and (76,
000,000 on a nominal $200,000,000 of Govern
ment 4 per cents, worth about $66,0<X>,""-)
in the London market.
Thus Spain has borrowed 111'i.000.000, for
which she sacrifices her revenues and
promises to pay $240,000,000, besides inter
est. The sum thus secured will last
barely four months from April, or say
till September 1.
In the meantime, in order to secure even
these small l.Â«>antÂ». Spain has permitted the
bank to increase its lssu<' of depreciated
notes so enormously that financiers every
where regard the collapse of the bank i"t
self as an impending event, and so close
ly are the bank's affairs and the national
finances connected that the bank's col
lapse would involve that of the Treasury
it has so long and so painfully supported.
From the first every financier in Eu
rope has seen clearly that Spain could not
carry on war with the United States with
out invoking financial ruin. Now every
financier sees that Spain cannot long con
tinue the war even at that risk. The time
draws near when she win not be able to
buy food or arms or ammunition for her
troops or to pay for their transportation
fr<>m one point to another.
So the financiers and bankers and busi
ness men of Spain are added . to the list
of those who call upon the Ministry
make peace as the only means of prevent
ing universal bankruptcy throughout the
kingdom.â New York World.

nized by the County Committee.
A special meeting of the Dewey Cen

evening at the headquarters in the Co

miniouÃly ignored and their request de-

the Republican party of San Francisco

and nominate its personal friends to be

nores the voice of the district club or
ganization: and whereas, the Dewey

and county of San Francisco, who are at

San Jose Primary Frauds.
SAN JOSE, Aug. 17.â The Good Gov
ernment Club is gathering evidence to
substantiate its charges of fraud at the
recent Republican primaries in this
county. Affidavits are coming in as to
ballot-box stuffing and illegal voting of
Democrats and the members of the or
ganization are confident that its dele
gates will be seated. The Good Govern
ment League delegates will go to the
State convention and make a hard fight
for their seats.
The Good Government League denies
the report published that the organiza
tion would fuse with the Democrats
and Populists. The matter has never
been discussed and no steps have been
taken looking to that end. If the State
convention sustains its appeal the
league will put a ticket in the field.
The Rea forces are disturbed over the
coming contest in tht State convention.
The election officers have been coached
as to their little story and affidavits
are being prepared. Rea says his dele
gates will be seated and denounces the
Good Government people in vigorous
terms. A good many of the candidates
disappointed at the Republican conven
tion are coming out independent, and in
this the "boss" sees defeat for some of
his pets. Altogether things are not
bright for the gang in Santa Clara
County this fall. There will be consid
erable opposition to the Rea ticket and
the better class of voters are deserting
the party. If this state of affairs con
tinues the Republican party cannot win
in Santa Clara County and the defeat
may cost the party the Governor.

SANTA BARBARA FLAGS
FLYING AT HALF-MAST
SANTA BARBARA. Aug. 17.â Flags
are flying at half-mast over all public
buildings in this city to-day out of re
spect to the late Hon. Rowland
Hazard, who died at Watklns, N. V..
last evening. Mr. Hazard was the son
of Dr. J. G. Hazard of political
economy fame.
For fifty years Mr. Hazard had been
at the head of the Peacedale Manufac
turing Company of Rhode Island, a
great woolen manufactory. He was
also largely interested in salt and
nnwrter works. He was always an
ardent Republican, deeply interested in
111 matters pertaining to the welfare
of the people. Mr. Hazard came to
Santa Barbara in 1886 and purchased
the Mission Hill property, upon which
he built a beautiful residence wnich he
SL occupied for eight months In the
?Xr ever since. He has been a warm
friend to Santa Barbara, and has done
Sore than any other one man in en
couraging public Improvements. Mr.
HnraS was the first president of the
Sfwer carnivals. He was their chief
promoter, and their success has been
due solely to his personal and financial
efforts He was called the "Father of
the California Carnivals." Mr. Hazard
f?aves an estate valued at $3,000,000.
Two -ons and three daughters survive
him. He was 70 years old.

the passenger facilities of the road. Next

tire, El Campo will prove a source of

GILLESPIE
AND CATOR
Secretary of the Pop
ulists Criticizes the
Anti-Fusionist.
Says That His Lai Is Bad and
Charges Shanahan With
Being Maudlin.
SACRAMENTO, Aug. 17.â T0 the
Editor of The Callâ Sir: We have
recently been favored by published
interviews, manifestos, letters of accept
ance, etc., from the so-called "middle-of
the road" Populists, probably the most
important of which was from the Hon.
Thomas V. Cator, who, to all appear
ances, has been appointed by that august
body to do the mandibular portion of la
bor incident to a campaign of good mor
als, good law, sweet brotherly affection
and pure political methods. While I have
every respect for Mr. Cator as an able
attorney at law and a perfect gentleman
in social affairs, I feel it not only my
privilege but my duty to compare his
present attitude with his past political
record from a Populist standpoint; there
fore I beg leave to answer through the
columns of your valuable paper a few of
the statements enunciated by that gentle
| man.
Mr. Cator says: "The question that
I will arise when the certificates of nomi
! nation of the two Populist State conven
i tions are presented for filing is not new
and. In my judgment, is not a matter of
doubt. The law defines a convention as
an organized assemblage of delegates rep-
I resenting a political party, when they are
! sent under a call to nominate a ticket
! for such party. The assemblage of del
! egates represents such political party if
i it nominates as candidates members of
i the party, hut if such assemblage of dele
' gates. Instead of so doing, appoints con
| Terences and divides up the ticket and
nominates members of other political par-
ties, then from that moment its acts are
ultra vires. The assemblage then mis
represents the party which it came to
represent. If no protest is made its ac
tion may go unquestioned, but if those
who protest against the selling of the
party to fusion withdraw and organize
and protest by nominating a straight
ticket of members of such party, then
they become the assemblage meant by
the law and good political morals, and
both in law and in morals are entitled
to the legal designation of the party.
This matter has been so decided by the
courts.
"Fusion or the blending and melting
away of a party is destructive of a party.
It destroys the purpose of its members,
which is to organize a party of men hav
ing a common political creed, determined
If possible to be governed by officers who
are members of that body and who nave
been adherents thereof. Fusion is what
we call against good morals, politically
speakingâ a fraud â upon the consistency
of a partyâ and it is not doubtful that all
honest officers of the law and courts will
decide against a thing so fraudulent and
degrading as permitting an assemblage
sent to nominate a party ticket to enter
into an agreement to do the contrary and
place its political opponents upon Its
ticket. The protesting delegates become
the true convention."
It is very apparent that the above inter
view was simply calculated to inculcate
in the minds of some honest and well
meaning people who have set up an inde
pendent ticket that their ticket was the
only real Populist ticket in the field, while
In "fact they must petition to get on the
ballot, and then only as independents and
not as Populists. Mr. Cator pretends to
reason from analogy, but there is no
analogy. It is an unalterable rule In
logic that when analogy fails the con
clusion falls also. Some people nave a
habit of quoting just so much of law
as suits their purpose and then filling In
with their own individual o-inions, as
suming that the public will believe it is
all law instead of some one's personal de
sire that It should be the law. The fol
lowing is the exact language, a portion
of which the gentleman quoted, ppges 16
and 17. California Election Law, IS<*6:
"Section 1180â Any convention as here
inafter defined had for the purpose of
making nominations for public office, and
also electors, to the number hereinafter
specified, may nominate candidates for
public office to be filled by election within
the State. A convention within the mean
ing of this act is an organized assemblage
of delegates representing a party which
at the last election before the holding of
such convention polled at least 3 per cent
of the entire vote of the State, county,
district or other political division for
which the nominntion is made; provided
(hat in any county, township, city or dis
trict wherein no general election shall
have been held after Its organization a
convention of any party polling at least
3 per cent of the votes east In the precinct
composing such county, township, city or
district shall have the same power and
Its nominations the same effect as though
such county, township, city or district has
been organized before the next preceding
general election." â . .
There is nothing in the law which pre
scribes any metnod by which a man's
political views must be obtained before
he can receive the nomination of a con
vention That matter is left entirely to
the convention Itself, and it has a right
to nominate any citizen who it believes
represents its principles. A political con
vention representing a party constituency
must bo acknowledged as sovereign, or
our devotion to Populist principles is
blown away by a breath.
The facts of the matter are simply
these: A committee of gentlemen came
from the Democratic State Central Com
mittee, and also a committee from tne
Silver Republicans, to wait upon the com
mittee of the People's party, fluly select
ed by the convention for the purpose of
forming a plan upon which all who agree
upon certain opportune fundamental prin
ciples could unite at the polls against
those who are determined to despoil tne
producers of wealth and who have al
ways sought to divide the reform forces
in order to conquer them, but it was
thoroughly understood that these com
mittees, or the committee as a whole, had
no power to bind the actions of their re
spective conventions; it was simply a
moral agreement that they would try and
prevail upon their respective party con
ventions to see the wisdom of unifying
their forces. No part of this plan pro
vided that the Populists should nominate
any Democrat for Governor; on the con
trary, the Democrats did not favor nomi
nating a Governor at that time. But the
Populist Convention fully realized that
there were many well-known Southern
Pacific attorneys and lobbyists who were
assiduously opposing the candidacy of the
Hon. James G. Maguire for Governor, and
delays were dangerous, for he was the
man wanted above all others, therefore
the convention arose in itÃ might, threw
aside all other matters, and nominated
the Hon. James G. Maguire, our next
Governor.
This was done because he was univer
sally regarded as an honest man, invul
nerable to any attack of corruption, be
cause he is a statesman of experience and
tried ability, because he was known to be
a Jeffersonian Democrat in the very first
rank, because we knew, through hi si rec
ord, that he was the very embodiment of
Populistic principles, hence his nomina
tion by the State Populist Convention
would, before the people of California,
offer the strongest living lesson in behalf
of the principles set forth in the platform
of the People's party. True Populism
means to do good, and in order to do good
we must be broad-minded and not hide
bound. Parties are valuable only as they
are the unselfish handmaids of patriot
ism. We must nominate men who live
above the fog in public duty and in pri
vate thinking, not more of large profes
sion and little deeds, but men who can
stand before a demagogue and damn his
treacherous flatteries without winking;
men who will be true representatives of
the people and not mere cringing tools for
corporations.
Mr. Cator says: "Fusion is what we
call against good morals, polltically
speakinj? a fraud, upon the constituency
of a party."
"Oh! Consistency thou art a jewel !"'
Had the gentleman discovered the
"fraudulent," "degrading, " "Immorality"
"of sellng out the party to fusion" while
he was a candidate for office his present
attitude would be entirely consistent and
worthy of consideration. But since he is
no longer a candidate his ethics may
be questioned and requires a more elab
orate explanation.
During- the campaign of 1596 Mr. Cator
called most all the leading Populists of
San Francisco together and delivered a
lecture to them. He asked, in order to
help his candidacy, that he be allowed
to name the entire legislative ticket of
Snn Francisco. This was conceded. He
did select and name all the members
for the Legislature that the Populists
nominated in said city. Only one straight
Populist was chosen. His reasons for
so doing were that a straight Populist
I could not be elected, hence Democrats
. were nominated who agreed to vote for
him for United States Senator. This was
all done for principle, because we thought
( that If Mr. Cator could be elected he
j would be a power in the United States
Senate to champion the people's rights.
But Mr. Cator now says It was a "sell
out and a fraud" upon his constituency.
If fusion means a fraud and a sell-out its
' definition has not been changed since the
campaign of 1896. Was it a fraud and
cell-out when Mr. Cator ran for free
holder in the city of San Francisco in
j December, 1896, upon a fusion ticket
composed of Republicans, Democrats
and Populists, without any common plat-
I form to stand or run upon?
He traveled over a â¢â¢'""ere portion of
this State and persuaded the county or
ganizations of the Populist party to do
largely as we had done. Anything from
Poundmaster to Judge of the Superior
; Court was sacrificed and traded off for
â  the Legislative ticket In order to send
! Mr. Cator to the United States Senate
j Mr. Cator returned from the National
I Democratic Convention at Chicago via
I the St -Louis Populist party convention.
IHe said that the national Democratic
I platform was a substantial victory for
Populist principles, and he stuck to that
statement until after the election of 1896
when he came to the conclusion that he
could not be elected to the United States
Senate, and subsequently wrote a letter
releasing future Populist conventions
from nominating him for that office
During that glorious campaign of 1896
Mr. Cator was a fusionist for Ponullst
principles: but who would have thought
that fn 1898. when the Hon. James G
Maguire was nominated to advance Pon'
ulist principles. Mr. Cator would become a
retromlngent? He has done and still pro
poses to do all In his power to prevent
an honorable union of the reform forces
but his efforts will be nil.
The propagator of fusion microbes in
1896 might do well to have some card's of
triangular shape printed for the campaign
of 189 S. with the magical words "\bra"
cadabra" on the one side and "Middle of
the road" on the other, to be worn around
the neck of the sanctified middle-of-the
roader. These.together with a liberal use
of Abraham's balm, would do much to
overcome the epidemic of 1896.
Mr. Cator now claims that the flftv men
who organized a sham convention at Sac
ramento are the only Simon-pure middle
of-the-road Populists. That depends on
what kind of a road it is. If it has steel
rails on each side, why. of course, we can
understand the ties that bind them to
gether for "good morals." "good law"
and "sweet brotherly affection." I am
not an attorney-at-law, but it seems to
me that it is "good morals," "good law"
and "good sense" to abide by the rule of
the majority and not by the rule of the
minority. Where the majority of a con
vention favors a certain line of procedure
and a minority oppose, yet take part by
moving resolutions, voting on same, as
sist In framing a platform, and vote to
adopt the same, nominate candidates,
fight, plead, vote and die. politically
speaking, and that because they were
honestly and fairly outvoted through
Populist means â majority ruled â that mi
nority does tne baby act and commits
political suicide by going through the
rurce of holding a convention. â¢
Notwithstanding they could only rake
and scrape fifty men, they proceeded to
nominate T. W. H. Shanahan, the arch
aic sycamore of Shasta, another Demo
crat. What did you say? Another Dem
ocrat? Yes, sir, that is what I said; a
Democrat. Not a Democrat of the Ma
guire type, in the first rank of reform,
but an old-fashioned Democrat of the
Bourbon type.
How did Shanahan get into the Popu
list party? Oh. you see it was like this:
The Populist party had carried Shasta
County by an enormous majority and
headed off Shanahan's political aspira
tions from a Democratic standpoint, and
he thought that it was about time to get
in out of the wet, so he went to the Popu
list party with an appointment card for
membership in one hand and an applica
tion for political office in the other. He
was accepted Into the party and elected
to the office. Therefore Mr. Shanahan is
now a Populist in the front rank of the
bolters' convention, and charges all who
opposed his candidacy for Governor with
having used corrupt methods. When he
came before the Populist convention
seeking the nomination for Governor
from that body previous to any division
or so-called bolt In the party he was in
a maudlin condition. Notwithstanding
that fact he attemnted to plead his own
case. He touchingly and pathetically
spoke of his numerous virtues and the
many reasons why the Populists should
nominate him for Governor. Among other
achievements he claimed to be the author
of the Australian ballot law.
"This must have been the hallucination
of the transcriber" (Addison*. Every
one who is familiar with the fight for the
Australian ballot law knows that the
grange, a farmers' organization and tne
trades and labor organizations were the
initiators of said law In this State. They
worked in conjunction with the tiÂ° n -
James G. Maguire, who prepared almost
the entire bill as It finally passed the Leg
islature, with the exception of a few
amendments. Mr. McGlashan of Truc
kee, Nevada County, seeing that his
friend, Mr. Shanahan. was getting into
deep water, thought to help him out by
changing the subject, so asked Mr. Shan
ahan a question: "If you receive the
nomination for Governor from this ko,
ulist convention, will you agree not to
come down off the ticket in the interest
of any other candidate for that office?
Mr. Shanahan floundered for a time and
then said: "That depends upon who
gets control of the party organiza
tion." Or in other words, his answer
was of such a nature as to leave the im
pression that if Governor Budd should be
renominated by the Democratic party for
Governor, he (Shanahan) would come off
of the Populist ticket, leaving it without
a head, but if Maguire should receive the
Democratic nomination, then Shanahan
would stay up and try to defeat the little
giant.
Now, I do not blame Mr. Shanahan for
trying to help his friend and benefactor,
Governor Budd, who appointed Mr. Shan
ahan as Code Commissioner at about
four thousand dollars a year. But I should
most certainly blame myself if I had in
any way assisted him to decapitate the
People's party to advance any such a
proposition, and I am surprised that any
true Populist cannot see through a veil
so thin. Talk about your middle-of-the
road Populism! If you mean the road that
leads to a victory for Populist principles
along lines of the least resistance, that
is just where I stand since having been
converted to that method by Mr. Cator
in 1896. and I am not only willing, but
anxious, to have every man, who will ac
cept our platform, be he Jeffersonian
Democrat, or Abraham Lincoln Repub
lican, go right along the middle-of-the
road with me. This convention, bear in
mind, was riveted and king-bolted by a
completion to a great extent of the func
tions which it was delegated to perform.
Just before Mr. Shanahan's address a
resolution was before the house, and Mr.
Mason of Santa Clara introduced a sub
stitute for the same, which was agreed to
by Mr. Cator and unanimously adopted
by the convention. By this resolution
every man in the convention was honest
ly bound to abide by the determination of
the convention in the selection of a nom
inee for Governor; the purport of this res
olution was simply this, any man was eli
gible to nomination in said Ponulist Con
vention who would stand on the platform
of the People's party adopted at that
time, signifying his assent thereto within
fifteen days after reaching the State.
There are a few people in this State who
think that Populism is some kind of ef
fervescence which they keep corked up in
a small bottle and carry in their inside
pockets, and if you want any Populism
you must apply to them and you can hayÂ«
a little on one condition only, and that is,
if you nominate them for office, but if you
don't agree to do that you cannot have
any Populism, so they say, because they
are going to vote the Republican ticket.
I say this advisedly, because several of
their leaders have said as much;, and it
might be well for some of the honest but
misled Populists to analyze that subject.
Mr. Cator. who has never lost a case, is
going to bring the matter before the Su
preme Court and have that principle of
majority rule declared unconstitutional,
and thereby give the rump convention
the legal People's party designation upon
the official ballot.
If the coun should decide that the ma-
I joritv which nominated the Hon. James
i G. Maguire for Governor is the legal Peo
ple's party and entitled to the designation
upon the official ballot, the question will
be settled, then, there and forever. But
suppose the court should decide that the
bolters were entitled to the designation
upon the official ballot, what would be the
result?
For the sake of argument we will sup
pose the bolters had disagreed among
themselves; then the minority would rule;
they would hold two conventions of bolt
ers and occupy two halls. Then suppose
that each faction of the bolters would
have another bolt: it would require four
halls for the bolters, then eight, then six
teen, and so on. until all the halls in
Sacramento would be occupied by bolt
ing bolters. We would naturally suppose
that when they got to that point they
would stop, but the way of the bolter is
peculiar and they bolt again, and hold
their bolting meetings on the street cor
ners, but finally the disagreement in
creases to such an extent that each bolter
of the bolters holds a bolter convention,
composed of one bolter in each bolter's
convention and that bolter himself. He
now has time for reflection and begins
to think how much better it would havo
i been for him if he had stayed with the
majority. He feels pleased at his actions
and wants to go back, but one part of
himself says no, never, no. The other
part says yes. now is the time to go back;
so he argues the case with himself and
cannot agree with himself and so bolts
from himself, then resolves himself into
atoms and each atom being a natural
born bolter, disappears into space, leaving
no evidence upon which the court could
base a decision.
This is. of course, a ridiculous supposi
tion when carried to the extreme, but,
nevertheless, right in line with what
would happen in the future if the court
should make such a decision. It would
establish a precedent dangerous to so
ciety, in the end it would bring about a
disregard for all law established by ma
jority rule and install a system of anarchy
by the minority. No party could hold a
convention but there would be a dissatis
fied minority which would set ltsolf ap as
the only true representative of such party
and the result would be chaos ;md ruin
to all political organizations. In such a
state of things where is Populism, which
is simply an English translation Ot Dem
ocracy ? Â Â , ,
If the State should provide by law
against the union, fusion, or coalescence
of parties who come to agree on certain
vital measures necessary to the welfare
of the people, it would legislate not for
its own good, but would enact :-tatvites for
its injury and ruin. In every law made
by the State it contemplates its welfare
and avoids all acts that contravene the
intersts of the people, therefore, Mr. fa
tor's construction of the law is revolting
to patriotism and a stigma on common
sense GEORGE D. GILIESPIE.
State Secretary of tno People's Party.
"Will Be a Candidate Again.
At the last State election Trowbridge
H Ward was elected Clerk of the Su
preme Court, receiving 120.007 votes. His
closest competitor was Peter McGlade,
on the Democratic ticket, who received
50.542 votes. Mr. Ward's plurality was
30.465.
Previous to that time Mr. Ward had
served four years as Clerk of the Su
perior Court of Los Angeles County,
and the ability and trustworthiness he
had manifested there was a good indi
cation that the important office of Clerk
of the Supreme Court would also be ably
filled. These expectations have been fully
realized during the term of four years
he has held office, and Mr. Ward has the
satisfaction of knowing that he is in
dorsed for renomination not only by all
those Republicans who voted for him be
fore, but by all the Judges of the Su
preme Court and by every lawyer irre
spective of party who has met him in
the course of his professional practice
before the Supreme Bench.
There are 109 women to every 100 men
in the world.

CALL HEADQUARTERS. OAK
LAND, Aug. 17. â Nobody was disap
pointed to-day. An old-time, hot pri
mary had been prophesied and the
prophecy was fulfilled. No one spoiled
for a fight, because before the spoiling
point was reached a fight was in prog
ress. In the districts where excitement
and gore are common on election days
the old reputation was well sustained.
San Francisco contributed its share to
the events of the day, and In one in
stance a certain Mahoney had en
gineered his schemes so well that he
was serving on a polling board in the
Fifth Ward before he was discovered.
Ward heelers and rock rollers were
conspicuous in certain precincts, and
that individual known as the Banjo
eyed Kid was in evidence In West Oak
land with a little following from San
Francisco. But they were watched so
closely that it is not believed they got
a chance to vote.
In the Fifth Ward, where the kid
glove element in politics is supposed to
reside, a little piece of work was dis
covered soon after the polls opened that
created a stiff breeze. The proper offi
cers should have been W. N. Vander
mark, A. W. Bishop, C. A. Scofleld and
J. Cal Ewing. When Mr. Bishop ap
peared he was told that he was too late
and that the board had been reorgan
ized. Judge Nusbaumer and Victor Met
calf went to investigate and found Mr.
Mahoney of San Francisco, Sam Ham
mer of the Third Ward and two other*
acting as officers. Hammer and Ma
honey were thrown out and A. W.
Bishop and Ed Johnston were substi
tuted. Clarence Crowell, the new,
county committeeman from the ward,
was sent for and disclaimed all knowl
edge of the affair.
In the First W T ard Metcalfs men wera
very numerous and claimed everything
in sight but the Hilborn attendants
lost no tricks. Some of the precincts ia
the west end of town polled an enor
mous vote, greater than was polled last
Saturday.
In the Fourth Ward Barney Nolan,
the leader of the Sporting Life gang,
quickly got into a row with a Metcalf
ticket peddler named Getchell. The
men mixed for several minutes and
both were severely punished. The lead
er of Mr. Hilborn's interests in this
ward was Mike Kelley, an employe of
the Mint, who was doing his best to
show his gratitude to Mr. Hilborn for
his appointment. Will Church. Rod
Church, W. L. Sinclair, Miles Doody
and Ed Duffy were there to offset the
Hilborn support and throughout the
day the Fourth Ward was a scene of
continued strife, argument and battle.
Hilb< m has always claimed the Sev
enth. Ward, as it Is his place of resi
dence, and it was generally conceded
by the Metcalf forces that the Con
gressman had much support in the
Eastern district. For several weeks
Judge Nusbaumer has been steadily or
ganizing and canvassing in that ward,
and to-day Frank Jordan, W. H. Mc-
Grath, C. F. Pugh, W. Walker and S.
Deasy are among those who were try
ing to offset Mr. Hilborn's vote.
Yank Savage and a few other ama
teur prizefighters made things lively in
the sanguinary Sixth. Everybody In
this ward is a politician, and strange to
say, it doesn't matter whether the pri
mary be Republican or Democratic, the
Sixth is always a battle ground from
the time the polls open until they close.
The precinct booth at the Galindo Ho
ttl has produced more fights than any
other precinct in the city, and it Is a
peculiar fact that there is always a
vacant store next to the hotel when
a primary is held. To-day Frank Bar
nett, John Rohan, Dan Doody, William
Keating, Ed Noblett and Edward Far
rell were working like slaves for Met
calf and were opposed in the interests
of Hilborn by Councilman Watkinson,
C. H. Ingler, Leslie Blackburn, Frank
Courant and Fred Murdock. Voting
was going on in this precinct all day,
but one of the officers guaranteed that
no one had voted more than three
times.
The Second Ward polled a good vote,
although throughout the day it was
evident that neither party was getting
a large majority. Nearly 400 votes were
cast in one precinct, but as this Is the
largest ward in the city the vote was
not so heavy. Mortimer Smith, R. O.
Johnson, Conrad Tisch and William
Reuter were the busiest of the Metcalf
supporters, while Councilman Dr. Gir
ard, Dr. Bodle, Mike Hallahan and Dr.
W P. Hall worked hard for Hilborn.
In the Interior districts the voting:
was very heavy. At Irvington the vote
was the heaviest known for years, and.
78
as is usual in country places, it was
' known that nearly three-quarters of
the entire vote were for â Metcalf before
they: were counted. Centerville polled
a heavy vote, but at Niles the election
was quiet. Pleasanton turned out
, strongly, and at Decoto and Alvarado
' the polls were particularly lively. Hay
wards also polled a big vote, and it was ,
noted that the Htlbbrn supporters were
in the majority.
The primary was certainly an expen
sive one for the principals. Both can
didates put up expensive organizations,
maintained large headquarters, did an
enormous amount of printing and sup
â¢ plied an unprecedentedly large num
ber of vehicles. Even the badges were
of an expensive nature being large,
and, in the case of Metcalf, beautifully
ornamented with a handsome satin
â¢ American flag. It was noticed that the
carriages were not blanketed as usual
with the names of the candidates, and
this was explained by one of the mana
gers on the ground that many conser
vative people liked to be driven to the
polls, but during the trip did not care
to be advertised as ; being the supporter
of any particular man.

mor.e than are necessary for a choice

Corbett. The service of the Roman
Catholic church will be held over the

\V. Montelth and Judge Maguire:
To the Hon. James G. Maguire, Golden
Eagle Hotelâ My Dear Sir: A little over
a month ago the question of effecting the
union of the anti-monopoly forces against
the railroad was before the Populist con
vention, and while conscious that a num
ber of other Populists should ordinarily
oppose any abandonment of our party
independence, joined hands to bring
about such a union, upon the definite un
derstanding that It was to be purely for
the purpose of fighting the railroad mo
nopoly, carrying with It the idea, that it
should be an honest and earnest effort
In that direction, and that no other out
side Influences should be permitted to
dominate the movement.
The proposition to nominate you for
Governor, you being a Democrat, and op
posed to us on some vital auestlons, was
not only a radical one. but an expediency

ginning to be directed to the
Congressional and other district
nominees.
It seems to be the general opin
ion that the fusion committee of
seven will report in favor of
making district nominations at
this convention.
In the Sixth Congressional Dis
trict much has been said con
cerning the candidacy of Hon.
Charles A. Barlcrw, the in
cumbent. An unfounded rumor
has been afloat to the effect that
he would not receive the Demo
cratic nomination. For various
reasons this has not been jub
licly denied. I believe the Demo
crats are honorable and true in
this matter and complete fusion
will be effected. In the People's
party he has a high standing.
One of the stipulations of the
northern counties in the Sixth
Congressional District was his
unqualified indorsement by the
People's party convention. This
the Honorable Mr. Barlow re
received unanimously and a com
mittee was appointed to draft
resolutions commending his Con
gressional career.
The people in his district are
unanimous in saying that he has
made the best Congressman
since the district was organized.
His results in the San Pedro har
bor matter won for him many
friends in the south. Hon. Mar
ion de Vries in the Second and
Hem. C. H. Castle in the Seventh
Congressional District will be re
nominated without opposition.
Every action of these esteemed
gentlemen meets with approba
tion from the people of the en
tire State.
MILTON CARLSON,
Chairman Populist Ceneral Com

Democratic party to do likewise by leav

At this stage of the proceedings the
Populist convention was so evenly divided
that it would have taken but little to
have turned the tide against you, and
there is no possible doubt that at that
moment had my friends adopted the tac
tics of a political highwayman, we could

This they did not do, and I went to
your rescue at a critical moment as un
selfishly and enthusiastically as 1 best
knew how, and as a result placed you
where you are to-day, reasonably certain
of achieving the goal of your ambition.
By this action as Populists we compelled
the Democratic machine to be just to
you and to give you the nomination you
not only want, but that It was right that
you should have, as an effective position i
in the battle against the monopoly. When
fusion was being discussed, we frankly j
toiii one of your Democratic friends that
1 should expect to be the fusion nominee ]
for Congress in the Fifth District. I ex- >
acted no pledges, but simply trusted to j
your sense of gratitude, together with
your spirit of chivalry' to do for me in
a small way as much as I had done for
you in a larga way.
Now the case, is exactly reversed. I am ;
confronted with the same class of fac
titious opposition in my own party, which
I believe to be stimulated by the same In
fluence that promoted the opposition to :
you In the â¢Democratic party, before we :
of the Populist party made such an oppo- !
sition useless, and that Is our common
enemy the Southern Pacific monopoly.
They sought to defeat you by dividing the
Democratic bosses and machine men. so
that they could prevent your nomination.
We saw the situation and we promptly ;
came to your rescue by absolutely refus- I
Ing to accept any other candidate but
yourself. , ' .. '< .
The present Congressional situation is
about this: The Democrats are willing to
concede the Populists three Congressmen, i
and if the Fifth District was conceded I j
could easily be the nominee. -There is
practically no candidate of any standing
in the anti-railroad fight against me. Mr.
Craig is purely a negative quantity. \\ ere
you to simply say that you wanted me
to be the nominee the matter would be
settled in five minutes in my favor; but
In our conversation Just had you politely |
but diplomatically stated that if I can get
my committee to stand for me, yours
would then support me.
Had I said the same thing a month ago
you would never have' been the Populist
nominee, but I went further. . I said flatly j
that you and no one else should be the I
fusion nominee, and I fought your, Dem
ocratic committee one whole afternoon
and finally forced them to that position.
I simply saw that the railroad could de
feat you among your own people, and I
came to your rescue instantly and with
all the vigor and determination of my
nature To ,come to my rescue involves
no such conflict, no bitterness of feeling
and no after reproach. All you have got
to do is to send for the members of the
two committees and tell them that you
desire that I be the. nominee, and I will
be the nominee within forty-eight hours.
The argument used against me Is that
you want Craig and don't want me. In
short, that Craig is the programme can
didate. I can hardly believe this; scarce
ly can I conceive that you woulu thus
strike the hand glady held forth to save
you from the machinations of the ma
chine in your party, whe;j you were in
grave danger," for I feel that when the
machine men in my party similarly situ
ate me I should not have to beg you to do
half as much for me. â¢ ' , :/
Now, Judge Maguire, I lay this matter
before you plainly and earnestly. I shall
not even demand of you the reciprocal as
sistance I require at this moment. I have
the battle nearly won, and your helping
hand will save mo, and.it is for you to
say whether you will hold it out. or let
me sink. This is something that. appeals
to your manhood, to your sense of grati
tude and to your purpose to be just. Will
you or will you not assist me? I leave it
to you and your conscience to decide
whether you will or not. Please let me
know by "bearer. Sincerely Yours.
Know uy GE ORGE W. MONTKITH.

Sunnyside Wants a Tunnel.
The property-owners in the Sunnyslde
district are In hopes that the Supervisors
will, when making the next tax levy, in
clude a sufficient amount to cover the ex
pense of running a tunnel under the
Southern Pacific Company's railroad
track at the junction of Bosworth and
Berkshire streets. This improvement, if
made, would give a straight roadway
1 from San Jose and Mission roads to the
northern portion of the city on the west
ern slope and to Golden Gate Park.

last evening. The occasion was a very
pleasant one. _

NOT FOR
CONGRESS
Boss McNab Slips Up
on His Ambitious
Dream.
Wants a Complimentary Nom
ination to Tell How it
Feels.
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MJSNTO, Aug. 17.â Gavin McNab has
awakened to the fact that he is in dis
favor even among the men he leads. The
Fourth Congressional District delegates
will get together some time during the
day and it is expected that th< y will
take some definite action in regard to
selecting a nominee.
Mi Nab, however, win not be the choice.
But he does not intend to let the public
know that he has worked himself into
such bad odor with his own delegation
that he cannot secure the prize. The sit
uation was made plain when McNab and
the few friends he has left began to cir
culate among the delegates late to-night,
asking- them to stand by him to the ex
tent of giving him a nomination which
he faithfully promises to decline. He
says he is not a serious candidate, but
the experiment of nominating him is re
garded as dangerous. He might want to
stick.
UNIT RULE.
Gaffey of los Angeles Tried to Tie Up
the Delegation for McNab.
CALL HEADQUARTERS, Sacramento,
Aug. 17. â The Los Angeles delegation in
reference to the report of the committee
on credentials pursued a most extraor
dinary course this morning which aroused
many members of that delegation, made ]
up as it is Of numerous fighters and ob
jectors. A caucus of the delegation was j
called for 9 o'clock in one of the Supreme j
Court rooms of the Capitol building, but I
sufficient notice had not been given to
every member and the result was that
there were numerous absentees.
Nevertheless thÂ« cavuus was called to
order by the chairman, Dr. Hill, and the !
members present proceeded to transact j
business by which, as they construed it,
they bound every member of the Los
Angeled delegation to vote as a tfnit on
the Moor of the convention.
The report of the committee on creden- I
tials came up for action by the caucus,
and a motion was made that the delega
tion vote as a unit for the majority re- j
port of the committee on credentials.
This motion was rushed through, aa per
programme of the reformers, by v viva
vo^,. vote.
John T. Gaffey, a prominent supporter
of Mr. Patton for chairman and one of
the McNab sympathizers on the delega
tion, stated that he thought that the cau
cus had made, a mistake. He pointed out
the fact that the report of the committee
on credentials had not as yet been sub
mitted to the convention, and the caucus
had as yet, no official information ;is to
what that report was. Furthermore, he
argued that it was unwise for the dele
gation to vote on the report until the del
egates had had an opportunity to listen
to the arguments to be presented on the
| floor of tne convention, pro and on, on
j the autocracy of the Democratic party, as
; now Illustrated by the present conditions
in San Francisco.
Mr. Gaffey moved to reconsider the vote
by which the delegation was bound to the
support of the majority report of the com
mittee on credentials.
Mr. Ramish of Los Angeles protested
against the action taken, as did also Dele
gate A. W. Fisher. They both announced
that they were opposed to tying up the
delegation until argument had been heard.
They made the point that it was like a
jury deciding a case before hearing the
evidence.
The war was on, and it looked as though
there would be a general .squabble. But
G. C. Wright poured oil on the troubled
waters by stating to the caucus as a
member ofuhu committee on credentials
that while he was in favor of seating the-
McNab delegation, he, as a member of
that committee, proposed to submit to the
general convention a resolution directing
i the organization in San Francisco in the
' future to hold primaries for the election
! of all delegates to the State Convention.
i A motion was made that the caucus cast
: eighty-one votes for the proposition.
The uolnt of order was raised that in
asmuch as the resolution suggested by
Judge Wright had not yet been drawn up
! the caucus could not act upon it. and.
furthermore, that no action could be
taken until the resolution had been pre
sented to the convention.
The chairman ruled the point of order
I well taken. His ruling was in direct op
! position to the action the caucus had
I taken on the report of the committee on
! credentials which had not been presented,
yet the committee had acted upon it.
In the meantime stray delegates began
to arrive and a great howl went up over
the action of the caucus in seeking to
*>md the entire delegation to the support
i of McNab. -
A motion to adjourn until 7 p. m. < hair
â  man Hill declared carried, but it did not
i end the fight. _ ,
Delegates Ramish, Fisher, O'i3rien, Rob
! erts and numerous others declared their
intention of refusing to abide by the de
cision of the caucus. They claimed that
I delegates Simons, Gaffey and others had
j shown bad faith in refusing to support
Dockweiler for chairman after he had
been fairly declared to be the choice of
i the Los Angeles caucus. Following this
precedent they declared the unit rule not
in force and agreed among themselves
I to present the Question to the convention
when the vote of Los Angeles was called
on the question of adopting the report of
the committee on credentials.

Exceeded Their Power.
The Supreme Court has decided that
boards of supervisors have no power to
tax owners* of property in municipalities
for the improvement of county roads.
This decision was in the case of J. H. De
vine against the Supervisors of Sacra
mento County. The Legislature of 1896-7
passed an act for the construction of a
highway of road between Sacramento city
and Folsom, the material to be rock
crushed by the convicts at the Folsom
Prison. No provision was made for the
money necessary for the construction and
the County Supervisors ordered a special
election to pass upon the issuance 01
bonds for $75,000, and in time they pro
ceeded to levy a tax to creat an >n tere / l
and sinking fund. This was resisted b>
Devine, who brought action in â¢ie su
perior Court for a writ of prohibition
which- was issued. The Supreme Court
upheld the Judgment, holding that tne
County Government Act provided for tne
Jurisdiction of the Supervisors and tnat
their power did not extend over the pun
lie work of a municipality. On this the
Supervisors are prohibited from collect
ing a tax from property owners WE a
municipality for road work in the coun
try, either "directly or indirectly.

President of the White Cross Society
Visiting Washington â Army-
Orders and Pensions.
WASHINGTON, Aug. 17.â Mrs. Jane
Creighton of Portland, Or., is in Wash
ington. Mrs. Creighton is president of

visiting the larger cities of the country
and will visit San Francisco on her re
turn home. The society is entirely-
American and is organized for the pur -
pose of relieving sick and wounded
American soldiers and sailors. Sena
tors McÃride and Thurston and Rep
resentative Ellis of Oregon are taking
a great interest in its work. Mrs.
Creightun called on President McKinley
to-day.

TWO STEAMERS REACH
VICTORIA FROM ALASKA
One Hundred Passengers Bring Half
a Million in Gold and $300,
000 in Drafts.
VICTORIA. B. C, Aug. 17. â The
steamer Danube arrived this afternoon
from St. Michael with 100 passengers
and a considerable amount of gold
dust. A party of Norwegians is said
to have $200,000.
The purser of the Danube says he
estimates the amount of gold brought
down on the steamer at half a million.
The drafts, he thought, would reach
$300,000. A large number of passengers
had small sacks and wallets ranging
from $1000 to $5000.
Passengers from the Ipwer Yukon
say a large number of men are going
up the Kyoku River to Tramway Creek
and other diggings at the head of that
river. They are taking out gold there,
but not in very large quantities. At
Mlnook all who have properties are
working energetically and looking for
ward to good results.
The steamer Govp- Stoneman is
lying on her side on a sandbar near the
mouth of the river and cannot be float
ed without considerable labor and ex
pense. The steamers Progreso and
Laurada were at St. Michaels -when the
Danube left, preparing* to come down.
The steamer Roanoke from Seattle ar
rived just as the Danube sailed.
The steamer Tees arrived to-day from
Skaguay, Alaska, Her passengers con
firm the news of the rich strike on Pine
Creek in the Lake Tagish district.
HUNDRED THOUSAND
TO BE MUSTERED OUT
Volunteers Will Be Permitted to
Choose as to Remaining in
the Service.
WASHINGTON, Aug. 17.â At mid
night to-night the President announced
his decision to muster out of the ser
vice from 75,000 to 100,000 of the volun
teers. Those to be discharged will in
clude the three branches of the service
âinfantry, artillery' and cavalry.
From a prominent official of the ad
ministration it is understood to be the
desire of the President to ascertain the
wishes of the volunteer troops them
selves as to remaining in the service.
One or two organizations have already
indicated a desire to leave the service
I as soon as the Government can rea-
I sonably do without them. They are
| composed largely of business and work
ing men whose private interests are
suffering by reason of their absence
from home.
It may be some time before the or
ganizations to be mustered out will be
designated, but the reduction in the
volunteer force will be made as soon
as practicable.

THROTJGH A TRESTLE.
At Least Six Persons Killed on a Brit-
ish Columbia Road.
VICTORIA, B. C Aug. 17.â News was
received here to-day of an accident on
the Union Colliery Railway line, result
ing in the death of at least six per
sons. The wires to Union City are
down and full details have not been re
ceived. As far as can be learned the
regular work train, with a passenger
couch attached, plunged through the
trestle which spans the Trent River
about midway between Union wharf
and Union City. It was about 125 feet
high and 500 feet long.

NEW YORK, Aug. 17.â The police
were so positive that in Dr. Samuel J.

the West Thirtieth-street station made i
an affidavit in the West Side Police j
Court to-day charging Kennedy with
homicide. He was held without bail for
examination on Friday.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, AUGUST 18, 1898.
COL, SMITH LOST
HIS REGIMENT
Funny Incident Before
Manila.
FALSE ALARM AT NIGHT
CALIFORNIANS SENT OUT TO
REPEL ATTACK.
Their Commander Returning From
a Solitary Reconnoissance Finds
His Entire Force Missing
From Camp.
Special Correspondence of The Call.
GAMP DEWET, with the Second
Brigade before Manila, July 21. â There
are now nearly 5000 men in camp under
General Greene, a force strong: enough
to hold its position in the con
tingency of a Spanish sortie. And
speaking of Spanish sorties reminds me
of a rather funny occurrence night be
fore last, which for a time looked as
though it might have a grimly tragic
side. You will have heard something
about it, of course, by caWe. This is
the story:
There were two battalions of the
First California in camp, and the en
tire First Colorado, the California boys
lying closer to the Spanish lines and
about two miles away. The Colorado
men had not been here as long as the
Californians, and their officers did not
understand the situation so well. Col
onel Smith, for example, knows what
the Spanish and the Tagalles are in the
habit of shooting back and forth across
their respective lines every morning
and evening, not, apparently, so much
for its purpose of killing each other as
to convince each that the other is at
attention in the trenches. These skir
mishes, as a rule, mean so little that
the Americans down here have ceased
to pause when commencing to count the
shots, and, although a Spaniard or a
Filipino is shot and killed occasionally,
that seems rather in the nature of acci
dent.
To return to my tale. The officers
of the Colorado regiment, being tender
feet, were not case-hardened to this fir
ing and, being ambitious, threw a line
of outposts toward the Spanish posts
well beyond the California pickets.
Matters were moving with the usual
routine in camp on Wednesday even
ing at 8 o'clock. Colonel Smith, watch
ful as usual, had been out to see how
the Spanish line looked at night with
a view to handling his men with I
greater effectiveness after dark. This !
is a country wherein men do not fight j
in the daytime, nature doing all the â 
necessary heating while the sun is at i
meridian, and, as the boys say, the col
onel was never slow in getting "next."
The boys in the shelter tent dozed
peacefully, and the officers about head
quarters gossiped of the relative
merits .of the Springfield and the
Mauser rifles as a dealer of death. The
cruiser Boston, sent over by Admiral
Dewey to cover the landing of Ameri
can troops, lay under the trees lining
the beach, covering: Manila with the
threat of her guns. The American
camp, Camp Dewey, It should be re
marked in passing, is well within the
range of the Spanish guns. It is not
shelled for the very good reason that
Augusti understands perfectly that one
shell thrown among the troops will
bring upon Manila swift destruction
from the fleet.
To return once more and finally to
my story. The camp dozed peacefully.
The big guns of the Spanish and
Aguinaldo's old smooth-bores at Pasay
growled sullenly across the trenches,
the growling broken, now and again,
by the sharp snap of the Mauser, like
the quick barking of terriers. Sud
denly into the peaceful scene was in
jected an element of disturbance. A
man in a blue uniform, panting like a
tired dog, rushed down the Calle Real
(King's road) from Pasay, tried to rush
the California sentry, and being halted
could gasp only the one word, "Dis
patches!" Then he caught his breath j
and told how the Spaniards had made |
a sortie, driven the insurgents from
their works and were coming down the
road at a double to attack the Ameri
can camp. Then the dispatch bearer
ran on to the Colorado camp.
There was the wildest commotion at
once. The whole peaceful aspect was
changed. In fact the only really peace
ful place was at the trenches, where
Colonel Smith was calmly watching the
firing on both sides, thinking how much j
better his boys would do if given a j
chance. A few insurgents had run back
past him, panic stricken for a moment,
but had quickly advanced again. He
was at the one place where nothing
was happening.
In camp Lieutenant Colonel Duboce
turned out the regiment quickly, tak
ing a strong position on a line from the
Calle Real to the beach. Colonel Hale
of the First Colorado turned out his
men with equal promptness, his left
resting on the California troops and
his right extended in the trees lining
the cross road from the Calle Real to
Pasay. It was magnificent, though
dark, but it was practice drill rather
than war. General Greene, having his
headquarters at that time on board the
China, was sent for hastily and came
ashore with his staff. Colonel Smith,
returning from the front in a caramat
ta rode peacefully into camp and
found his regiment missing. That is
! not as small a matter as it seems at
first glance. A thousand Americans is
a pretty large quantity in this land of
mangoes and small men. The colonel
found his boys presently, agreed to the
disposition of them which had been
made and waited in the darkness for
the coming of the Spaniards he had not
seen at the front. The Spaniards that
they waited for never came. And that
is the true story of the first battle be
fore Manila.
Late this afternoon the Insurgents
captured five Spanish spies at the out
posts of Pasay. They had passes rolled
up in cigarettes. They were taken to
â Racoor and will be shot in the morn-
[ ng . SOL N. SHERIDAN.

FREQUENT SKIRMISHES
BEFORE THE SURRENDER
Captured Spaniards Report That the
Enemy Suffered Heavy-
Losses.
Bpeclal Cable to The Call and the New York
Herald. Copyrighted, 1898, by James Gor
don Bennett.
BY SOL N. SHERIDAN.
CAMP DEWEY, Aug. 4 (via Hong
kong, Aug. IT). â I spent last night with
the troops in the outworks, in a piti
less tropical rain. I found that there
has been firing nightly. A man was
killed in the breastworks on Mon
day and another by a shell from
Malate Tuesday night. The Span
iards since Tuesday have made
no attempt to break through
the American lines. The dogs feeding
on the dead on Wednesday were prob
ably eating the bodies of the Spanish
slain in Sunday's battle. Traces of
these horrid feasts were found by the
American troops on the field yester
day.
The men are highly excited over Sun
day's events. The troops have per
mitted their ardor for battle to out
run discretion and have been firing,
especially Tuesday night, largely at
shadows.
The trenches were held last night by
the Second Battalion of the First Cali
fornia, Major Sime commanding, and
a battalion of the Third Artillery,
Major O'Hara commanding. Both are
cool officers and had orders not to fire a
shot until the Spaniards were coming
over the earthworks. Throughout the
night I heard only two shots from
Mausers, sharpshooters spotting our
pickets' right flank through loopholes.
I saw moving shadows in the moonlight
and dogs straying about the battle
field, but not a sign of Spaniards. Not
a shot was fired from the American
lines, consequently the Malate guns
were silent.
Captain Richter succumbed to his
wounds to-day.
August 6.â The Spaniards renewed
the attack on the American outworks
last night. The trenches were occupied
by a battalion each of the Fourteenth
and Twenty-third regulars and the Ne
braska volunteers. The latter, holding
the extreme right, and the company of
regulars on the extreme left, returned
the Spanish fire, and hot firing lasted
a half-hour. The American casualties
were chiefly from shells. The killed
I were:
Privates McCann, Company C, Four
teenth Infantry.
S. F. Howell, Company D, Fourteenth
Infantry.
C. Saur, Company F, Twenty-third
Infantry.
Eleven were wounded, four seriously.
They are: Privates D. J. O'Connor,
Company D, thigh; Clinton Lambert,
Company E, chest; Lucius Snow,
Company D, Fourteenth, leg; Claus F.
Head, Company G, abdomen.
During a heavy artillery duel this
| forenoon the Utah artillery replied
i with spirited fire to the Spanish guns,
j finally landing a shell near Pulverin.
It must have disabled the guns, as the
Spanish ceased immediately.
Corporal Ernest Dozier of the Signal
Corps has been promoted to sergeant
by General Greene for distinguished
gallantry on the field. During the heavy
artillery fire last night the telegraph
line in the trenches was damaged. Do
zier repaired it under fire with great
sangfroid, enabling communication to
be maintained with headquarters at a
Itical moment.
August 9.â Three deserters from thr
anish army, brought in from the
nches by the First Colorado, report
enty Spaniards killed, including a
?taln and two lieutenants, and six
:n wounded in the engagement on
|"'y 31. Six were killed and ten wound
on August 2. An officer was killed
[ six men wounded on August 5.
n English merchant refugee informs
that shells from the Utah battery
ered the walled city on July 31. One
iloded in the Malate magazine, kill
three officers, another exploded in
Englishman's residence near the
sjlish Club, another entered the in
try barracks at Malate, exploding
the, rrtTdst of a company, killing an
cer and several men. He says no
reliable statement of the Spanish losses
are obtainable, but they were heavy.
Deserters report the Spanish position
before Malate, Santa Ana and Paco
greatly strengthened, additional guns
having been mounted.
August 10â To-day Father William D.
McKinnon, chaplain of the Californ
ians walked along the beach from the
camp into Manila. About 500 yards
from Malate he was fired on by a Span
ish picket, but was uninjured. The
priest walked bravely forward and was
met by two captains and escorted to
the Malate fortress. Father McKin
non later was escorted to Archbishop !
Nozaledas at the palace, where he was
received cordially. The Archbishop
stated he always had been anxious for j
a restoration of peace, and would do all
in his power to secure a cessation of
hostilities. He did not think Manila
would be surrendered without a fight.
The Spaniards in the city are starving,
nevertheless. They expect Jaudemes
to make a last desperate effort.
The Archbishop denied most emphat
ically the authorship of the circular as
cribed to him. exhorting the Spaniards
to resist Yankee invaders with their
last drop of blood. As a man of God,
he said, he could not have given utter
ance to such sentiments. He always
had been an apostle of peace. He bade
Father McKinnon a cordial adieu,
placed a carriage at his disposal for the
visit to Jaudemes, whom he described
as small and of not imposing presence.
The priest received a c<-rdial welcome,
but the Governor's conversation was
less peaceable than the Archbishop's.
Manila Jaudemes said, would not be
given up without a struggle. He had
received General Merritt's and Admiral
Deweys notification that the city would
be bombarded and the Spanish Hag still
flew. If the Americans wanted the city
they must capture it.
After this interview Father McKin
non was driven in a carriage along the
Luneta and escorted across the lines
by Spanish officers. His courageo-us
act, after having been fired on during
a previous attempt, the bullet cutting
his clothes, was one of the most re
markable of modern warfare.
Private Edward A. O'Neill of Com
pany H, First California, was acci
dentally killed to-day by Private John
Lunsford of the same company.
The Astor Battery is anxious to get
to the front before the surrender of Ma
nila, having now plenty of ammunition
fit for service.
Private Edwin L. Young of Astoria,
Company A, First Oregon; Private Hol
brook, Company C, First Oregon, and
Leslie B. Paden, Company E, Minne
sota died of typhoid fever.

Philippine Revenue Tariffs.
WASHINGTON, Aug. 17.â The War
Department has issued regulations for
the guidance of officers concerned in
the collection of duties on imports and
exports, taxes and other charges and
exactions to be collected as a military
contribution at ports and places in the
possession or under the control of the
forces of the United States in the Phil
ippines. The regulations are substan
tially the same as were recently put
into operation in Cuba. The rates of
duty, however, differ in many import
ant particulars.

THREE AGED LECHERS
ARRESTED AT TACOMA
By Use of Money and Candy They
Accomplish the Ruin of Lit
tle Girls.
TACOMA, Aug. 17.â Tacama is stirrÂ«d
up over the arrest to-day of three men
over BO years of age, charged with as
saulting girls under 13 years of age. The
police are after a fourth member of the
gang. Public feeling runs high, de
manding that these alleged despoilers
shall be severely punished if found
guilty.
Six girls between 11 and 13 years old
have so far confessed to doings with
members oflthe gang. Early this week
the mother of one of the girls discov
ered the awful truth. Upon being ques
tioned the child confessed, naming the
men now accused, and also naming
other girls who had been her compan
ions.
The mother hastened to Justice Mat
tison with her story. Prosecutor Far
ley was summoned and an investigation
fallowed. Several of the girls were
called in. At first they denied the
truth of the story, but finally broks
down under Farley's questions and told
all.
So far as known the crimes charged
were committed between July 20 and
August 9. The men arrested are Thomas
Peters, aged 67, an engineer; Charles
Byer, a shingle mill employe, and Sam
uel Holloway, a colored barber, each
over 50 years of age. B;;th Ho-lloway
and Peters have children of their own.
Peters' wife and daughter are heart
broken over to-day's developments. The
girls implicated are members of good
families. The defendants have been
placed in the County Jail and their ex
aminations set for Friday and Tuesday
next.
