GOLDEN STATE BOYS
FOUGHT GALLANTLY
HOW MANILA
WAS TAKEN BY
OUR FORCES
Appreciation of the Hu
mane Action of the
Americans.
Californians First to Hoist the
Stars and Stripes Within
the Spanish Trenches,
HONGKONG, Aug. 17.â Manila was
surrendered, after a nominal defense,
on Saturday August 13. The American
flag now flies over the capital of the
Philippines and this was accomplished
without great expenditure of life. I
vas one of the first who traversed the
availed city.
The Manila newspapers on August 5
published the news that Captain Gen
eral Augustl has been superseded by
â¢Begiinno Cabo Don Fermin jaudemes
'Alvarez, and referred in terms of con
tempt to the Yankees. On August 7
Admiral Dewey and General Merritt,
acting jointly, notified General Jau
demes that they might attack the city
forty-eight hours after his receipt of
their note, and gave him an opportunity
to remove all non-combatants. This
joint notification was carried by Lieu
tenant Armitage, of H. M. S. Immor
i'alite.
General Jaudemes acknowledged Its
receipt at 12:30 p. m.. and expressed his
thanks to the American commanders
for their humane sentiments. He add
ed, however, that, finding himself sur
rounded by insurrectionary forces, he
was without any place of refuge for the
Increased number of wounded, sick and
women and children who were within
the walls. No request was made that
the Amerioans provide for their safety.
Foreign warships with refugees
. aboard moved out of the harbor on the
morning of August 9. A small party
of foreigners, chiefly British, remained
In the suburban portions of the city.
The Concord anci Petrel lay off the
mouth of the Pasig in such positions
as to prevent any vessels escaping, but
no action occurred until August 13, the
delay being to allow the American
troops to extend their front on the
right of the line. Frequent visits by
the Belgian Consul in the meantime to
General Merritt and Admiral Dewey
led to rumors that terms of surrender ]
yere being arranged .
. . Our fleet began to move In at 9 j
o'clock on Saturday morning, Dewey's j
flagship, the Olympia, led the fighting j
line as of old. The Olympia opened j
â  with her 8-inch guns at 9:35, the first
four shells being directed against the
Malate fort, called San Antonio de
Abad. All of these first shells fell short.
A passing rain squall made it difficult
. to get the range properly and observe
the result of the shots. The Raleigh,
Petrel and Callao also opened on the
.fort, the Boston, Baltimore, Charleston
eind Monterey standing in as supports.
.Most of the firing was done at a range j
of from three thousand to four thous
. and yards. The practice was excellent
: as soon as the range was determined, i
â¢! Most of the 5-inch shells fell in a bat
:â  tery protected by earthworks.
Sixteen 8-inch and sixty-nine 5-inch
DEWEY SENDS WORD
OF MANILA'S FALL.
WASHINGTON, Aug. 17.â The Government re
ceived the following direct official information to-day
of the surrender of Manila after a combined land and
sea bombardment last Saturday. It came in dis
patches from Admiral Dewey, brought from Manila to
Hongkong on the Zafiro:
"MANILA, Aug. 1 $.â Secretary of the Navy, Wash
ington: Manila surrendered to-day to the American
land and naval forces, after a combined attack. A
division of the squadron shelled the forts and intrench
ments at Malate, on the south side of the city, driving
back the enemy, our army advancing from that side
at the same time. The city surrendered about r;
o'clock, the American flag being hoisted by Lieutenant
Brumby. About 7000 prisoners were taken. The
squadron had no casualties, and none of the vessels
were injured.
"On August 7 General Merritt and I formally de
manded the surrender of the city, which the Spanish
Governor-General refused. DEWEY."
The Lieutenant Brumby mentioned is Admiral
Dewey's flag lieutenant.
shells were fired by the Olympia and
the Raleigh and the Petrel each drove
in about as many.
It was a pretty sight to see thi* Cal
lao. Lieutenant Tappan commanding,
and the launch Barcelo riding In the
heavy surf close Inshore pouring their
fire on the enemy's riflemen. There was
rifle fire in reply and the Callao was
struck. She was not damaged. The
Call-Herald launch followed close be
hind through the surf.
The general signal, "cease firing,"
was hoisted at thirty-two minutes past
10. Our infantry was seen a few
minutes later moving forward upon the
Spanish intrenchments. Their advance
was made under cover of a heavy fire
from the Utah Battery.
"With colors flying and bands playing
the troops moved swiftly along the
beach. There was the creek to be ford
ed. They plunged into it and were
soon across. Once over they deployed
in skirmish order along Malate, keep
ing up a heavy rifle fire and finally
halting on the runeta.
As far as I could see from The Call-
Herald launch the resistance made by
the Spanish troops was stubborn in the
extreme, at least for a nominal de
fense.
Flag Lieutenant Brumby went ashore
about noon in a Belgian launch, accom
panied by Inspector General "Whittier,
to find and interview General Jaudemes
and discuss the terms of the capitula
tion. General Merritt was present to
discuss the settling terms.
General Jaudemes was found after
considerable difficulty. He was finally
discovered in the security of a church
filled with women and children. . Flag
Lieutenant Brumby was forced to
speak sharply and peremptorily to sev
eral officers before he could find the
Governor General. Subsequently pro
ceedings regarding the terms were con
ducted in the municipal buildings, the
Governor General consenting to leave
the church after a brief conference.
The terms are briefly these:
The capitulation of the Philippines.
Officers to be allowed to retain their
swords and personal effects, but not
their horses, during their stay in
Manila.
Prisoners of war surrendering their
arms will have the necessary supplies
\ provided from the treasury. When
i that is exhausted the Americans are to
make provision.
All public property is surrendered.
The future disposition of Spanish
troops who surrendered will be deter
mined by negotiation between the re
spective governments.
Arms may be returned at General
Merritt's discretion.
The banks will continue to operate,
under existing regulations, which are
subject to change by the United States
Government.
Lieutenant Brumby, Immediately af
ter the terms of the capitulation had
been signed, hurried off to lower the
Spanish flag â in reality to lower all of
Spain's flags in the Philippines by tak-
THE SAN FBAKCISCO CALL, THTTBSDAY, AUGUST 18, 1898.
Ing down one. He was accompanied by
two signal mÂ»n from the Olympia.
This little party found Its way after
considerably difficulty into Fort San
dlago in the northern portion of the
walled city. There a large Spanish
flag was flying. Grouped about It were
many Spanish officers.
Brumby's presence there Jn the vic
tors' unifor i attracted a crowd from
the streets. They hissed as he ap
proached to haul down the flag. Then
the Stars 1 Stripes rose in place of
the other. Many of those present wept
bitterly as the flag of the victorious
stranger climbed Into place above the
fort.
Fearing the crowd might lower "Old
Glory," Lieutenant Brumby asked an
American Infantry officer to move up
a detachment to guard it. Fortunately
me met a company coming up with a
band. The Infantrymen presented
arms and the band played "The Star
Spangled Janner," which lent some
eclat to the ceremony.
The conduct of the Spaniards was
disgraceful after capitulation.
The gunboat Cebu was brought down
the river with a Spanish flag flying,
and was set on fire at the mouth of
the Pasig. A party of Americans
boarded her and hoisted the stars and
stripes. They tried fruitlessly to save
three launches and several boats,
which were also destroyed.
Landing soon after General Merritt I
traversed the walled city. I found both
inhabitants and soldiers looking re
markably well, considering the fact
that they had been reported to be
starving. Many were well pleased that
the capitulation of the city had been
agreed upon, as a bombardment of the
city proper must have been attended
by severe loss of life among women
and other non-combatants.
Our troops quickly occupied the city
on both sides of the Pasig. sleeping in
the streets throughout the night of
August 13, which was a wet one, and
made the strange conditions doubly
disagreeable. Yet the conduct of the
American troops was beyond praise. It
was simply admirable. They fraternized
good-humoredly with Spaniards and
natives.
A group of regulars squatted in Es
colta street, one of the principal busi
ness thoroughfares, edifying a great
crowd which had formed about them
with tuneful plantation ditties.
Our ships which were engaged
cruised freely at dead low water inside,
which on the British admiralty chart
is marked "three fathoms," although
the Olympia was drawing twenty-four
feet. As a matter of fact her navigator,
Lieutenant Calkins, during her stay
here carefully surveyed^ the water
along the city front.
The Callao went within rifle range
while covering the flank of the troops
as they advanced. The services of
Lieutenant Tappan, who is her com
mander, will doubtless receive especial
mention in the future.
The Monterey was not called upon to
try her guns during the bombardment,
but undoubtedly her presence and the
boldness with which she was navigated
within easy range of the city, had con
siderable influer cc on the Spanish in
their decision to capitulate.
The insurgents on August 14, the day
after the surrender, entered some Span
ish trenches on the outskirts, but were
repulsed. General Merritt notified them
that they would not be permitted to
come inside the city. It is probable the
Americans will now deal with the in
surgents, unless an amicable arrange
ment is arrived at soon.
The terms of the capitulation were
formally signed by the American Com-
missioners â General Green, Colonel
Whittier, Colonel Crowdes and Captain
Lambertonâ and the Spanish Commis-
sioners â QQlonels San Jose Maria La
guen, Felin Don Carlos Reye and Gen
eral Don Nicholas de la Pena y Cuellas.
H. M. S. Immortalite and Iphlgenia,
whose commanders kept them in mo
tion, watched the fight from favorable
positions.
Alter the American flag was hoisted,
the German warship Kaiserin Augusta
slipped out, presumably Jmund for
Hongkong, without the courtesy of of
fering to carry dispatches from Ma
nila.
The Americans moved out of camp
early on the morning of August 13,
General Anderson directing operations.
General Greene, with the left wing,
swept along upon the trenches before
Malate. General Mac Arthur led the
right wing with the Astor Battery,
which took up a position on the right
of the Paslg and did gallant work.
One instance of this was when a Span
ish block house was carried by men
using their revolvers. The only rapid
fire gun of the line was silenced by this
gallant advance.
Three men of the Astor Battery were
killed.
The hardest fighting of the day was
done at a point on the right wing,
where the guns of the fleet under Fight
ing Dewey could give no assistance.
After the fleet had raked their posi
tion the Colorados, supported by the
Eighteenth regulars and the Utah Bat
tery, Â«wept it with the deadliest of
fires. The Spaniards fell back before
the charging Colorados, who followed
them closely, giving them no rest until
their position was ours and the Ameri
can flag was raised/ by Californlans,
who had been charging with the Col
orados.
The Californians, who were subjected
to a galling fire from Spanish sharp
shooters in the houses on the right,
moved past the Colorados into the su
burb of Ermita, where Company L,
which was leading, engaged in hot
fighting along Calle Real, where the
Spaniards had occupied street barri
cades. Once Calle Real was cleared
the attack was virtually over. About
noon a white flag was floating over the
city walls.
The Californians advanced at double
quick across the Luneta as General
Greene and his staff arrived to receive
the surrender.
By some error, while the troops were
standing at rest, Spaniards in the
walled city fired, fatally wounding
Privates Dunsoupe and Lamerson of
the Californians.
Our casualties were eight killed and
forty wounded.
The Spanish loss is estimated at 120
to 600 killed and wounded.
The Americans captured 11,000 pris
oners, 7000 being Spanish regulars; 2000
Mauser rifles, 3000 Remingtons, 18 mod
em cannon and many of an obsolete
pattern.
INSTRUCTIONS SENT TO
MERRITT AND DEWEY
Must Preserve Law and Order and
Treat Insurgents and Span
iards Alike.
WASHINGTON, Aug. 17.â The two
American commanders at Manila, Ad
miral Dewey and General Merritt,
united hi a joint dispatch which was
received here late this afternoon, ask
ing for instructions as to the manner
of dealing with the various elements,
particularly the insurgents, now that
the city Is occupied by the American
forces. After a conference at the
White House, in which Secretary Al
ger and Acting Secretary Allen par
ticipated, instructions were sent to the
American commanders. The text of
the request for instructions and of the
answer was not made public, but Sec
retary Alger summed up the instruc
tions substantially as follows:
"The instructions are to enforce law
and order and to treat all law-abiding
citizens alike."
It had been thought In some quarters
here that the showing heretofore made
by the insurgents would lead to their
receiving a certain amount of recogni
tion in the way of joint action between
the United States forces and those of
the insurgents when the city was oc
cupied; but the instructions sent to
night are based on occupation by the
United States alone; and as a high ad
ministration oflieial summed up the sit
uation, "there shall be no joint action
except between Merritt and Dewey.
They will maintain law and order and
will treat insurgents and Spaniards
alike, requiring all to observe peace and
order."
It was note- that neither the first
dispatch of Admiral Dewey nor the
joint dispatch of Dewey and Merritt
made any reference whatever to the
part which the Gorman cruiser Kaiser
in Augusta took in removing Captain
General Augusti from Manila to Hong
kong. From this the officials believe
that the American commanders do not
attach serious significance to the course
of the German naval authorities. The
incident continues to cause some irrita
tion here, but it is practically certain
that no official cognizance will be taken
of it. A high official of the Navy De
partment said that although the course
of the German naval commander was
one of bad taste, there is no disposition
in any official quarter at present to
carry the incident to the point of con
sidering it a breach of neutrality, as
has been suggested in some British
newspapers.
AUGUSTI SAYS DEWEY
TOLD HIM TO ESCAPE
American Admiral Said to Have De
clared He Would Raise No
Objections.
NEW YORK, Aug. 17.â A dispatch to
the World says: August! claims that
he offered to surrender himself person
ally to Admiral Dewey ten days ag-o.
Dewey refused to accept his surrender
unless he surrerdered the city at the
same time. He told Augusti that if he
had a good chance to run he had no
objection. August! requested the Ger
mans to carry him to Hongkong. If
the Augurti story Is true it makes it
all the more mysterious why the Ger
man Consul denied to Consul General
Wildman all knowledge that Augrusti
was in Hongkong. He also denied that
the Kaiserin Augusta brought him.
The Philippine Junta is delighted with
the news, and delegation after delega
tion Is crowding the American Consul
ate tendering allegiance.
BERLIN, Aug. 17. â The Nord
Deutsche Allgemenine Zeitung explains
that the Kaiserin Augusta, which
brought away General Augusti from
Manila, left the harbor after the sur
render of the city and asserts thnt the
Spanish officers were allowed to go
free, so that General Augusti was able
to go whithor he pleased.
When Admiral yon Diedrichs permit
ted him to go to Hongkong on board the
Kaiserin Augusta, says the paper. It was
by agreement with the American com
mander-in-chief, if. indeed, the Ger
man admiral considered such an agree
ment fitlll necessary. Moreover, it is
also to be observed that the Spanish
troops at Manila may by mutual con
sent proceed to Spain or Hongkong.
MADRID. Aug. 17.â 1t is now assert
ed here that General Augusti left Man
ila after the surrender. The Govern
ment has received his report giving de
tails as to the surrender, but it is not
yet ready to publish It. It is asserted
that General Augusti on August 7 re
fused all propositions to surrender.

nila. Perfect order prevailed in Ma
nila on the evening of August 13. As
the Americans marched in guards were
placed around the houses of all for
eigners, in order to prevent their being
looted.
The insurgents were not allowed to
take part in the attack upon the city,
but were kept in the rear of the Ameri
cans. In order to prevent bloodshed
they were forbidden to enter the city
after the surrender unless they were
unarmed.
Before the surrender the Spaniards
burned the transport Cebu in the "Pa
sig River.
It is supposed that Admiral Dewey
was unaware of the departure of Gov
ernor General Augusti. The
officers first heard the news of the
Governor General's 'flight on their ar
rival at Hongkong. Augusti's escape is
considered to have been prearranged,
as he took with him his family and
his suite.
Army Captain Kills Himself.
WASHINGTON, Aug. 17.â The War
Department to-day received a dispatch
from Lakeland, Fla., stating that Cap
tain John Carmichael of the commis
sary department had committed sui
cide by shooting. His home is in Lex
ington, Va. He married a daughter of
John Randolph Tucker. When ap
pointed he was with the Nicaragua
Canal Commission. He had been ill for
some time.

has decided to reject the resignations

Cortes, it is asserted, will reassemble
on September 15.

DELAWARR MUST
PAY THE COSTS
Acquittal Practically a
Conviction.
THE EARL HELD BLAMABLE
WRONGFULLY SOUGHT TO IN
FLUENCE HOOLEY.
Not Wholly Absolved by the Court in
Connection Wilii the Ac
cusations of
Bribery.
Special Dispatch to The CaH.
LONDON, Aug. 17.â 1n the Queen's
Bench Division of the High Court of
Justice to-day Justice Wright acquit
ted Earl Delav.arr and Mr. Bradshaw
of the charge of contempt of court in
their alleged attempt to induce Ernest
Terah Hooley, the bankrupt company
promoter, to falsify his evidence in the
Bankruptcy Court. The learned Judge
said, however that both were blam
able and he mulcted them in costs.
Justice Wright said thxt while he
must absolve Earl Delawarr of the
charge of bribery, he must hold that
the Earl had wrongfully tried to induce
Mr. Hooley to correct some statements
made by him in the bankruptcy pro
ceedings and had wrongfully promised
to help Mrs. Hooley.
Mr. Bradshaw's evidence as to Earl
Delawarr's proposal to help Mrs. Hoo
ley was remarkable, although Mr.
Bradshaw had no cause to testify
falsely. It must be remembered, the
Judge continued, that the Earl had an
overwhelming motive for obtaining Mr.
Hooley's retraction.
He could not believe that it was out
of mere charity that Earl Delawarr
| had promised Mrs. Hooley Â£1000. On
| the contrary, he was forced to the con
clusion that the promise was connected
with the Earl's strong and natural de
sire to clear himself from Hooley's ac
cusations. Nevertheless, he acquitted
Delawarr of the imputation of in
ducing Mr. Hooley to testify falsely.
With regard to the alleged bribery,
he could not wholly absolve Earl Dela
warr. With regard to the money the
| Earl received, possibly the latter be
, lieved it was given him for services
j rendered or as a gift from Hooley. The
I Judge said he supposed that was gen
erally the way names were paid for,
but as these proceedings were only in
tended to vindicate justice, it would be
a sufficient punishment for Earl Dela
warr's indiscretion if the court ordered
him to pay costs.
With reference to Mr. Bradshaw's
case. Justice Wright considered him.
i he said, much more guilty in asking
! Mr. Hooley falsely to say that he made
a retraction without communicating
with the Earl Delawarr. Bradshaw
was guilty of subornation of perjury,
and while he (the Judge) was in doubt
whether he ought not to deal very dif
ferently with Mr. Bradshaw, he had
concluded to make the same order in
his case as in that of Earl Delawarr.
FREEMAN HALSTED STEPS
FROM A SPANISH PRISON
Call-Herald Correspondent Free at
Last, After Having Been
Sentenced to Death.
Special cable to The Call and the New York
Herald. Copyrighted, 1898. by James Gor
don Bennett.
SAN JUAN, Porto Rico, Aug. 17.â 1 was
released from prison this morning. I am
allowed full liberty. Captain General
Macias was chiefly instrumental in ob
taining my freedom, and Mr. Cordea, the
British Consul here, was unceasing in his
efforts in my behaj^^ HALSTED .
NEW YORK. Aug. 17.â Freeman Hal-
Kted was arrested in San Juan on March
25 last, charged with having taken photo
graphs of the fortifications there. He
was sentenced to nine years' imprison- !
ment. When the American fleet bom- j
barded San Juan on May 12, the Spanish I
authorities became so angry that Mr. :
Halsted and other political prisoners were i
sentenced to death. While being led from !
prison Halsted made a desperate resist
ance and his arm was injured. Using that i
accident as a pretext, the Spaniards j
spread the report that Halsted had been
hurt by the fire of the American fleet,
hoping thereby to avoid another bom
bardment.
Halsted 1b a British subject, and
through the efforts of the British Consul
the plan of taking the correspondent's j
life was abandoned and he was accorded [
special privileges in the presidio, where he
was confined.
BRING $ 100,000 IN DUST.
Three Small Steamers Reach Seattle
From Alaska.
SEATTLE. Aug. 17.â Three small steam
ers arrived here to-day from Alaska with
LEO passengers, and their stories of the
lights and shades of the Alaska c >ld
prospector's wife. About a third of them
only were included in the list of fortunate
ones. Their combined sacks of gold dust
are estimated at $100,000.
Advices from Pine Creek, near Lake
Tagish, where a rich strike was reported
a few days ago, say. that the. diggings are
not bo rich, as reported. The stampede,
however, continues, men even going from
Cooks Inlet and Copper. River. *â â¢' â¢ ' '.-'.
At St. Michael boats are landing freight
and passengers, and the congestion has
materially been relieved.- Provisions are
reported scarce and command high prices.
The steamer Hair arrived at Dawson
July 25 from St. Michael with a large
number of passengers and a cargo of
fresh supplies. '

To Expel Religious Communities.
Special Cable to The ("all nnd the NVw York

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, AUGUST 18, 1898.
FOREST FIRES
DEFY CONTROL
San Mateo's Mountains
Still Ablaze.
SCOHES OF MEN BACKFIRING
WIND MAKES THEIR EFFORTS
ABORTIVE.
Thousands of Acres Denuded, Several
Shingie Mills Consumed and
Travel Stopped by the
Extreme Heat.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
REDWOOD CITY, Aug. 16.â Forest fires
have been raging in the mountains above
Woodside since Friday last and are still
beyond control, although a force of sev
enty-five men have been kept continually
at work trying to check the flames. Yes
terday the men had them almost controll
ed, but a heavy wind set in and scattered
burning timbers in all directions for
hundreds of feet.
The fire started at what is known as
Borden & Hatch's mill, on the head of
Purissima Creek, and worked up to the
top of the mountain. It then took a
southerly course and has been making
headway ever since, until now an area
covering thousands of acres has been
swept clean, destroying thousands of
cords of wood.
Yesterday the fire reached a shingle
pile belonging to C. D. Hayward, con
taining about 400,000 shingles, which was
entirely consumed. Another mill belong
ing to the same man and containing
about 150,000 is now threatened and will
probably be burned.
Travel on the roads has been stopped,
owing to the great heat. On account of
the heavy winds and the great number
of pirie trees on fire it will be a difficult
matter to manage the fire. It is now
burning on the ridge back of the Jones
ranch, and efforts are being made to
keep it from coming down this side of
the mountain, for this ranch is covered
with brush and bark, it not having
been burned for two years past. If it
gets started on the dry brush there will
be no stopping it until it has done proba
bly thousands of dollars' worth of dam-
age-
Men are backfiring in the ' hope of
checking the flames, but as yet their
efforts have proved futile.
It is hoped that the heavy fogs which
are prevailing north of the fire will
reach the burning territory, and this at
present seems to be the only salvation.
UKIAH'S TASTE OF HADES.
Surrounded by Mountain Fires and
the Thermometer at 114.
I'KIAH, Aug. 17. â For a week past the
j mountains to the westward of this city
have been on nre, making at night a
grand, beautiful and picturesque scene.
i But while the artistic sense has been
â  gratified, a high price has been paid, on
â¢ account of the loss of timber and the dan
; ger to residents at the foot of the raoun-
I tains.
Friday night the fire swept with resist
1 less energy down what Is known as*Gibson
: Creek Canyon, destroying everything in
; its path and threatening the extinction of
; the San Francisco and North Pacific fish
j hatchery from which, owing to the enter
' prise of President A. W. Foster, so many
streams have been stocked.
As the fire burned down the ridge to
ward Doolan's Canyon the sky was con
stantly lighted up with a crimson tint.
At times the roaring could be plainly
heard in town, a distance of one and a
half miles. Fires are also burning in the
hills to the eastward.
These mountain conflagrations have
made the air of the valley thick and
murky with smoke, while the mercury
has climbed higher than for years. For
three days it stood at 112 degrees, and
one day at 114. A few days ago Eli Wens,
a well-known resident, was sunstruck,
and at one time his condition was re
! garded as serious. This is an occurrence
almost unprecedented in the history of
! the valley.
SURRENDERED AFTER
PEACE WAS DECLARED
Capture of Manila, However, Was
Not Contrary to the Rules
of War.
WASHINGTON, Aug. 17.â According
to Admiral Dewey's dispatch the sur
render of Manila occurred about 5 p. m.
last Saturday. The peace protocol was
signed at 4:23 p. m. on Friday. It is
said that the difference in time between
Manila and Washington is such that
the surrender, reckoned on Washing
ton time, would be about twelve hours
ahead of the time at Manila, or abcrut 5
o'clock Saturday morning. In point of
actual time, therefore, the signing of
the peace protocol preceded the actual
surrender of Manila by a few hours.
But it is a well-established rule of mili
tary law, accepted the world over, that
a peace armistice or cessation of hostili
ties doea not become binding upon com
manding officers until they receive ac
tual notice of it.
Under this rule Admiral Dev/ey and
General Merrltt would be bound by the
armistice only from the time the notice
at it reached them at Manila, This
could not have occurred on Saturday
prior to the bombardment, as the cable
was not working and no dispatch-boat
could have gotten through with the no
tice before Monday or Tuesday. In
short, it is agreed in all official quar
ters that the forcible taking of Manila
was entirely regular, and that the city
and bay is now held on the basis of
this military enforced taking rather
than on the basis of the mutual ar
rangement between the two countries.
This may become material Avhen the
question arises as to the future of the
Philippines, for the discussion is likely
to proceed from the American stand
point that Manila was taken by force
of arms and- is so occupied.
CHAMPION MIDDLE-WEIGHTS.
Olympic Club Seeking to Match Mc-
Coy and Tommy Ryan.
William Kennedy, superintendent of the
Olympic Club, wired to Kid McCoy and
Tommy Ryan yesterday, offering to
match them before the Olympic Club of
this city any time in September for the
middleweight championship of the world.
Kennedy also offers 60 per cent of the
cross gate receipts. â¢â .
Since the match between McCoy and
Corbett is considered off McCoy will l,e
left without a match on hia hands. Ryan
Is very anxious to meet McCoy again, but
it Is thought that McCoy is not quite so
strong in his desire to face Tommy Ryan
In the ring. The last time they fought
the battle was stopped in the fifth round
by the police when everything pointed in
Ryan's favor.

and Charles L. Taylor Jr. of- this city.

FOR COWARDICE
IN A SKIRMISH
Court- Martial Awaits an
Ohio Captain.
LOST HIS HEAD UNDER FIRE
RATTLED HIS MEN BY ORDER
ING A RETREAT.
After the Panic He Is Said to Have
Been Found Hiding in a
Culvert Under the
Roadway.
Bpeclal Cable to The Call and the New York
Herald. Copyrighted, 1898, by James Gor
don Bennett.
ARROYO. Porto Rico, Aug. 12 (by
Call-Herald dispatch-boat Sommers N.
Smith to Key West, Fla., Aug. 17).â
Captain Biddle of Company C, Fourth
Ohio Volunteers, is still here under ar
rest, awaiting court-martial for alleged
cowardice in a skirmish with the Span
ish a few miles out of Arroyo. Captain
Biddle's company beat an inglorious re
treat when they were on the skirmish
line and were attacked unexpectedly
by sharpshooters. The unexpectedness
erf the attack probably had much to do
with Captain Biddle losing his head and
the panic into which he threw his com
pany. As his men deployed as skir
mishers and were advancing the men
called back and forth and chaffed one
another on what they called their 'Tab
bit hunt." Suddenly there was a whiz,
a snapping of leaves, a splintering of
bark and little eruptions of dirt at the
boys" feet.
"Hello, rabbit! The rabbit is no
longer on the run!" exclaimed one ot
the soldiers. â¢ ,
Biddle's men were slipping behind
trees and bushes and preparing to make
a stand, when some one gave the com
mand to retreat.
"Run, boys, they are a thousand to
one," came the command, as the men
were preparing to make a brave re
sistance against their as yet invisible
foe. It is alleged that Captain Biddle
gave that command. Had this panicky
alarm not been given, and had nut the
men seen their commanding officer run
ning with his back to the fire, they
would have stood their ground and
fought just as bravely as they did when
they received their baptism of fire at
Guayama.
Captain Biddle, it is alleged, was
found hiding in a culvert under the
roadway.
CRUSHED BY A TRUCK.
Josephine Lindsey, Eight Years Old,
Run Over and Killed.
Josephine Lindsey, 8 years old, was run
over by a truck and instantly killed on
Folsom street, between Fifth and Sixth,
Tuesday evening. The driver of the truck,
Thomas J. Lyman, knew nothing of the
accident until after it occurred. Marcus
Ososki, a butcher at 905 Folsom street,
was a witness of the tragedy, and says
that the girl was playing on the truck.
Jumping off and on while it was in mo
tion. She lost her balance and fell be
tween the wheels.
Lyman was arrested and booked for
manslaughter at the Central Station by
Officer Minehan, but was released on his
own recognizance. The body of the child
was taken to the Morgue. Her father,
Alex Lindsey, is a truck driver, and lives
at 414% Clementina street.

A country boy visiting New York stopped a
frantic runaway team that was about to dash

year by Hoatetter's Stomach Bitters. People

brought back to good health by It. All the
sick should try it.

. Dried Peaches and Apricots firm.
. Prunes dull and nominal.

A ROSY PROSPECT.
BULLISH VIEWS OF A BANK
ER ON THE SITUATION.
The circular of Henry Clews, under date of
New York. Au S are strong ""V alues on the :
Stock Exchange are strong and advancing.
Peace developments were an Important st im.i
fus to the upward movement: but cheap â none>
and excellent business prospects hove been
much more potent influences and their effects
have been but partially realized. At : thÂ« mo
ment there Is a great plethora of idle funds
â¢eeklng investment, and money Is phe nomena lly
easy in all parts of the country. *â¢-?â¢*â¢
â  vestments are on a 3% to 4 per ce.U basis
while second-class stocks and bonds are mMm(
at figure which bring the Investor 6 per cent
and less. Holders would like more Profitable
Investments, but they are not to be had. As
' enterprise revives new opportunities will be of
fered and rates for money must advance. Our
n-w possessions-Cuba. Porto Rico and possl
blv the Philippines-will afford some new out
lets but these may easily be overdone; for
out s^ik^ -s^rS
very limited outlet for the energies of the
United States for some time to come Cheap
money Is always an Important aid to a bun
campaign, and two sources of supply encourage
belief in its abundance for a period of con-,
siderable length. The banks, aided by the new
3 per cent bonds, are likely to materially ex
pand their circulation as soon as rates for
money advance. The large Increase In gold
production and the prospect of gold Imports
also tend to ease in money and higher prices
In the Stock Market. to enlarge upon Im
'â¢lt is almost needless to enlarge upon im
proved business prospects, as these are under
: Stood by every intelligent observer, and readily
confirmed by those familiar with conditions In
the interior; especially In the wheat growing
and Iron trade sections. The August Govern
ment report indicates a yield of ?'Â»>eat amount
ing to 607.105.000 bushels, against oSO.OOO 000
actual last year, and a yield of corn amounting
to 1,896,000,000 bushels, compared with 1.902 -
000.000 bushels last year. As a whole the agri
cultural sections are enjoying prosperity, and
there is every prospect of Its continuance an
other year. Our industries are also rapidly
reviving, and their promise Is for continued
activity for some years to come: in fact, en
thusiasts anticipate a positive boom, which is
neither desirable nor probable yet. While the
railroads are doing a heavy trafficâ much of
it at a senseless sacrificeâ and putting them
selves In good financial condition, the indus
trials are coming to the front as profit makers
and scoring handsome advances. They offer
tempting inducements ,to the speculators, but
must be handled with discrimination. Trade
conditions favor larger earnings, but It must
be remembered that many of these concerns
are particularly exposed to new competition In
good times from establishments having lighter
capitalization and the most modern equipment.
We believe the Stock Market will reach a still
higher level, accompanied of course with the
usual setbacks." j_i_ â 
SUGAR AND TEA TRADE.
For the fiscal year ending July 30 the Im
ports of Sugar into the United States were
4.719,769.504 pounds raw. valued at $94,138,031,
and 199,136,109 pounds refined, valued at $4,928,
150. against Imports for the last fiscal year of
2.557.832.155. valued at $55,f07.525, and 100,997,866
pounds refined, valued at $2.434.57 J.
The Imports of tea into the United States In
July amounted to 5,240,146 pounds, valued at
$777*393. The New York Commercial says: "In
previous years, from 1894 to 1596. the Imports
of tea averaged nearly 8,000,000 pounds a month.
In 1597 they averaged nearly 9.500.000 pounds
monthly, but in 18SS not quite 6.000.000 pounds
a month. The average Imports since 1894 have
been about 94,000.000 pounds annually, or 7,-
SOO.OOO pounds monthly; therefore last month's
lrrfpotts were fully 2,000,000 pounds below the
normal quantity."

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECASTS:
The pressure is highest off the northern coast
Of California ard lowest in Southwestern Ari
zona. It has fallen over the entire Pacific
Coast, the greatest fall being In Central Cali
fornia.
The temperature has remained nearly sta
tionary In all districts. It Is slightly above the
normal in California.
The weather Is cloudy or partly cloudy along
the coast of Oregon and Washington, and clear
in other portions of the Pacific Slope.

hours ending midnight. August 18, 1S98:

EASTERN MARKETS.
NE"W YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK, Augr. 17.â The feature of the
day in the stock market was the action of the
directors of the Chicago. Burlington and Qulncy
In advancing the quarterly dividend on the
â tock from the 6 per cent rates. Although this
action has been rumored and has been indus
triously discounted in the advance of the ktoeka
for many days past and profits already taken
on the advance the announcement to-day was
met by a furious outburst of speculation in
that and all the other Granger stocks, which
carried the price of Burlington full 3 points
over the previous high level of the movement.
The action of the directors is accepted as in
dicative of their belief in the power of the
property to maintain earnings at last year's
high level. Intimations are given that the
forthcoming statement of July earnings will
show last year's large average maintained. The
statement of Bt. I'aul, second week in August,
Is corroborative of the confidence in the earn
ing power of the Grangers. The increase in
earnfngÃ over last year Is $36,210, and over 1892
(the largest previous corresponding week in tiie
road's history) the Increase in over $3000. Such
a showing by the roads most affected by the
present very light movement of grain la con
vincing evidence of the large general traffic
which the awakening of business Is bringi-u?
to the railroads. The renewed Impetus given
to the advance In prices wan a natural conse
quence. The Burlington news came on a yield
ing market, due to an enormous liquidation of
profit* on the recent advance. This realizing
had been going on for over an hour, to the
advantage of the very heavy outside demand
for stocks, and had resulted in the marketing
of upward of a third of a million shares of
stocks vilh only a fractional Impression on
prices, t lÂ»'ch were, however, at the low point,
vl th the exception of some of the industrials,
notably rubber, leather, cotton oil and stocks
of the Iron and steel companies. Burlington
bounded up from the low point about 5 points.
St. Paul 3V4 and each stock in the group ad
vanced sharply. After a period of rather fev
erish lluctuatlon the stocks settled down to a
firm ana quiet tone near the top. while the
urgent demand for stocks went seeking through
the list, advancing first one group and then
another. Scarcely a stock failed to move, even
the neglected and despised coalers coming in
for gains of 1 to 3 points. The only exceptions
to strength were the stocks advanced yesterday
and the day before, which were held back by
profit taking. The Northern Pacific and Union
Pacific stocks were conspicuous examples. A
squeeze In shorts In sugar and a shaking out
of weakly margined holdings In tobacco were
features of the late trading. Top prices were
clipped by profit taking before the c\oen in the
majority of cases, but the closing wan ptfady,
only slightly below the best. The casing of
the sterling exchange rate was probably due
to the falling off of buying of stocks in London
for New York account. Sales for London ac
count in this market continued large tor bonds
at higher priced. Total sales, J4.065.009. United
States 3' s, when Issued, advanced % in the
bid price. Government bonds were otherwise
unchanged.

LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK. Aug. 17.-The Evening Post's
London financial cablegram says: The stock
markets here were quiet to-day, but less firm.
The feature was the sharp fall in Spanish 4s
on heavy sales from Paris. An offered explana
tion was reported difficulty on the Paris
Bourse, but ; well-informed operators say this
Is not so.
I The fall In Spanish bonds was really not sur
i prising, the wonder being that the price has
I been so good. The continued purchase of silver
by Spain, stimulated by the regulations as to
the note issue by the Bank of Spain notably.
had a weakening effect on Spanish exchange on
London, and this weakness tends to react on
Spanish stocks. Americans were easy on realiz
ing but the closing was steady on New lork
support- Grand Trunk was better.
The Paris Bourse was dull on the fall in
Spanish 4s and DeÃeers. The Berlin market

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, Aug. 17.â Corn was the leader of
the markets to-day. Conditions have been
propitious for a recovery from the severe set
back which the Corn crop suffered in conse
quence of the late drought, and the present
outlook was said to be vastly more assuring.
The start was fairly steady at about previous
day's closing figures, but the strength did
not last long. Very soon it became apparent
that big lines of long Corn were for sale, and
that brought out smaller lots In large num
bers. At every slight reaction during the ces
sion offerings overwhelmed the buyers, so that
at last shorts that had been â willing to take
moderate profits held o't and deprived sellers
of their chief customers. The market con
tinued to decline right up to the close, Septem
ber I>>4c on the day.
Wheat was momentarily weak at the open
ing and then turned strong. The start for 3ep
/ tember was at from 65>4@65%c, compared with
65% c bid at the close yesterday. In the course
1 of an hour it rose to 65T4c and turned down
again from that point on account of weak

BOSTON WOOL MARKET.
BOSTON, Aug. 17.â The American Wool and
Cotton Reporter will say to-morrow of the
wool trade: Taking the market as a whole the
situation is very quiet. Everything now de
pends on the goods market, which still falls to
respond to expectations. The sales this week
may be considered surprisingly large, in view
of the talk of dull business which has been
heard on every Bide. As a matter of fact, how
ever, the total has been materallly enhanced
by a few large transactions. On point of num
bers the sales seem to be increasing from week
to week, but the fragmentary character of the
sales is also apparent. The sales of the week
In Boston amount to 2,025,000 pounds domestic
and 541 000 pounds foreign, making a total of
2 565 000. against a total of 3.339.000 for the pre
vious week and a total of 10.720,000 for the cor
responding week last year. Sales since Janu
ary 1 1838, amount to 74.593.810 pounds, against
237,187.600 pounds at this time last year.

though inferior fruit is cleaning up at low
prices. Plums arÂ« still dull and cheap.
Grapes are steady at the quotations. The
weekly report of the Weather Bureau reports
serious damage to the crop in the interior
valleys by the recent hot spell, but the coast
counties escaped without Injury.
Watermelons are firm, but the other melons
are lower and plentiful.
Limes have again gone up, being scarce.
Lemons rule firm, with light supplies.

THE STOCK MARKET.
Local securities were fairly active. The only
noteworthy change was a recovery in Oceanic
Steamship to $55.
There was nothing new in mining stocks.
The Oceanic Steamship Company has de
clared a monthly dividend of 50 cents per share,
payable September 1.
The South SwanHea Mining Company of Utah
has declared a dividend of 0 cents per share,
amounting to $7600.
The Standard Consolidated Mining Company
will pay a dividend of 10 cents per share,
amounting to $17,839, to-day. There are 178,394
shares of the company's utock outstanding.
The Excelsior Mining Company of. Nevada
County has levied an assessment of 1 cent per
share, delinquent September 15.
Ore from the Dexter mine at Tuscarora, Nev.,
recently shipped to Salt Lake City, averaged
$330 In gold and 16 ounces In silver to the ton.
The delinquency of the assessment of I*4
cettts per share on the Skagit Cumberland Coal
Company has been postponed to September 15,
and the day of sale to September 29.

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A brnnch of the United States Hydrographlc
Office located in the Merchants 1 Exchange, is I
maintained in San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
Navigators are cordially invited to visit the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail-
Ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
Information can always be. obtained regarding ,
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters ,
of interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street, is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon. 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Signal
Observatory. Mare Island. Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped ;
on time or giving the error, if any. is published
In the morning papers the following :?Â£>'â¢
Ensign (retired), U. 3. N., In charge.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides j
the early morning tides are given In the left i
hand column and the successive tides of the I
day In thp order of occurrence as to time. The j
second time column gives the second tide of j
the day, the third time column the third tide i
and the last or right hand rolumn gives the

fdvnn are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low w*ters.

MISCELLANEOUS..
A launch belonging to the United States
steamer Scandta, while out on a trial trip with
a number of invited guests, including Mrs.
Charles Paxton. Mrs. Cone, MrR. Peterson, Nat
, Messer. Captain Hart of the Scandia, the en
gineer and assistant and two sailors, was run
into by the United States steamer General Mc-
Dowell. All the occupants were rescued with
the exception of MrÃ. Paxton, who was
drowned.

COMMITTED
TWO MURDERS
He Killed His Uncle in
Arkansas.
WAS TRIED AND ACQUITTED
JIURDER OF ÃBY DOES NOT
SEEM TO WOEEY HIM.
The Prisoner, Who Will Set Up the
Plea of Self-Defense, Tells
of His Varied
Career.
James Nathaniel Meadors, who killed ,
Jonas Ury at Camp Barrett, could be j
tried, convicted, sentenced to death and j
exn-cuu-d by the military authorities if j
they so desired, as the murder was com
mitted in a military camp and the mur- !
derer and his victim being soldiers.
It is usual, however, in such affairs to
turn the prisoner over to the civil authori
ties, and this has been acme in this case.
Private George S. Fouts of Company C
fcwore to a complaint in Justice of the !
Peace Laurence's court in East Oakland
yesterday morning, charging Meadors
jprlfgi the murder of Fry.
The news of Try's death did not seem
to affect the murderer la the least, for he
. chatted plrasantly.
-. "I am not at all worried about this mat
: ter," he said. "I have done nothing that
Â»ny other man of nerve would not do un
der the same circumstances. All 1 a.^k is
that they push my trial along as fast as
possible, for I don't want to lie in this
devil's hole With all nationalities.
"On January 26, 1*75, I got into some
trouble with an uncle, F. M. Moore, in
Crawford County, Ark. He had come into
my house drunk, and intended killing my
only brother; but I killed Moore. I was
tried in my county and acquitted by
â â  twelve honorable jurymen. Of course,
that affair broke me all up: but I don t
. Intend that this Ury affair shall affect me
â¢ â  that way. Since I left Crawford my little
farm has been sold under foreclosure.
. JJy wife and 1 were born and raised U>
that neighborhood, and we have four chil
dren. I am a stranger here, and my
means are limited: but all that I have
Roes, and that's all I car. do. All I want
is an 'even break,' and I'll have that it
.. I have to fight for it. I haven't an attor
. ney as yet. and I am net worrying over
: the probable outcome of my case. I
: know that if the men who witnessed the
affair will come up and tell the truth I
will be cleared. I don't want any one to
testify to anything he cant swear to with
a clear conscience. _
"I know that in the sight of man. and
the creator I killed Try in self-defense. I
couldn't put up any other plea.
â¢â¢â Whr-n I left Crawford County I went
to Arizona and tried to recover damages
for the death of my son. who was killed
. In the mines fifteen miles north of Kins
man, but I failed, and last April I came
out to California to visit my relatives
at Petaluma. whom I hadn't seen since
I was a little boy. I found Tncle Peter
; Meadors had died, and so I stayed with
the family until I enlisted."
Meadors denied that the direct cause
of the stabbing was over the $3 he had
loaned Try.
â¢'lt was all on account of a nasty mess
of Try's in our t^nt." said he. "Try
blamed It m Joe Burnett, and when he
left it to me. I up and said 'you know
you did it. Try.' and he called me a vile
name, I am not at all sorry for what I
did. I would do It again. I can stand
a joke as well as any man, but this Ury j
: abused me all the time."
The feeling at Camp Barrett is very |
much against Meadors, and Company C I
. members feel particularly sore over the
murder, as they regard the affair a dis- ,
grace to their organization. He has j
no sympathizers, and the evidence !
against Meadors will be strong.
"If I am convicted," said Meadors. In
conclusion, "and am sentenced to be exe
cuted, I will take my medicine Like a
man. I do not dread it as much as I
would to swim across the bay."
A DISREPUTABLE FAMILY.
Mrs. Maria Wren and Her Daughter
Arrested for Being Insane.
: Mrs. Maria Wren, 56 Natoma street,
find her daughter Mar)' have been a nui
sance to the neighborhood of their home
/or a long time. Yesterday morning Mary,
>ho Is about 30 years of age, followed a
daughter of Hugh Donohue, a neighbor,
â¢who was wheeling a baby carriage, and
threatened to kill her. Donohue com
: plained to Captain Spillane, and Mary
: -was arrested for being insane by Ser
,'geant Coogan and Policeman Orr. She
was locked up in the insane ward in the
"'â¢ Receiving Hospital.
A few hours later Policeman Moriarity
went to the house to arrest th<- mother on
the same charge. She had locked all the
doors, and Moriarity had to crawl
through a window. She grabbed hold of
him as he was climbing through, and
Btruck him several times on the face be
fore he overpowered her. She has been
arrested times without number within tho
last seventeen years for drunkenness and
disorderly conduct, and is looked upon by
â¢ Captain Spillane as the most vicious wo
man he has ever encounter' -d.
The house occupied by the mother and
daughter was in a wretched condition
from filth, and their condition when on
the etreet was a perpetual eyesore to the
neighbors. Mrs. Wren has a son, a "dope
fiend," who is now serving time In San
Quentin.

For Open Asiatic Ports.
Isidor Jacobs, manner of the Califor
nia Canneries Company, yesterday sent a
letter to President Hugh Craig of the
Chamber of Commerce, urging that the
chamber take up the matter of keeping
the Asiatic ports open to the trade of the
â world. He declares that it is a matter of
no small importance to the mercantile in
terests of the- Pacific Coast, and says the
entire country should stand with England
to keep these ports open. Mr. Craig in
his answer said:
"Certainly it Is to the interests of the
Vnited States to stand in with England
and demand that ports In China shall be
kept open to the commerce of the world
while there is a possibility that Russia
18 attempting to gather under her control
tUI the larger portions of that empire."

STOLEN EGGS IN HER BOSOM.
The Mission Police Capture an Annoying
Kitchen-Door Thief.
The pilferer of cupboards has ai last been brought
to bay. The kitchen, the store rooms, the bread bas
kets and the milk cane of the Mission homes will no
longer be visited by the soft purloining hand of Liza
Farrell, at least not for some time, for she fs now 'gaz
ing through prison bars and awaiting the judgment of
the police courts. Detectives Graham and Fitzgerald
discovered the lady at the residence of Joseph F. K.
Carpenter, 550 Valencia street, on Tuesday afternoon,
and she was taken at once to the Seventeenth Street
Station, where she was charged with petty larceny.
"Now go easy, will you," she said to Detective Fita
gerald, as he searched her person for stolen edibles.
"Be careful, will you, or you'll smash those eggs.
Let 'em alone; I'll give 'em to yer, though it breaks me
heart," and Liza of the gentle touch reached into the
bosom of her drees and brought out a dozen of as fine
eggp as ever Leghorn laid. Fitzgerald ordered Liza to
take off her bonnet, which she did very reluctantly.
"Give me back me carrots," she cried, as the turnips,
carrots and other vegetables rolled out on the floor.
"Now, lady," the detective continued, with adaman
tine persistence, "unburden yourself."
"Not before you, sir. I want you to understand that
I will not disrobe before any and all of you. I didn't
rob you. What are you kicking at, I'd like to know,
anyway. Well, if I must, here goes." With the first
garment came a deluge of all things that Frenchmen,
Englishman or German ever dreamed of in his mad
vlsione of stomachic material. There, was a vegetable
garden, portions of a hennery, sections of the pig and a
bit of charlotte russe smeared around by way of deco
rations. Liza was mournful, for all other week's labor
was turned to naught, and there she stood in her de
parted glory, surrounded by the fruits of her labors.
For some months the kitchens and outstanding cup
hoards of the Mission houses have suffered from the
depredations of this woman. One of the most constant
losers is J. Hanna, 186 Lexington avenue. Mr. Hanna
has noticed during the past few weeks an appreciable
diminution of the milk in the can left out over night,
and an occasional absence of a loaf of bread. Being
great of heart, he thought it was due to some poor,
destitute and hungry creature who wished to stave off
starvation by appropriating milk and bread on occa
sions. Butâ Mr. Hanna changed his tack the other
morning when he went out to thecupbo.ard to get him
self a bone of ham. The, ham was gone, and Mr.
Hanna said something. Pursuing his investigations, he
discovered that what was a well-filled larder the night
before had been transformed into a complete vacuum.
The next night he waited for the poor destitute prowl
er, but he waited in vain. This waiting pastime was
Indulged in for the two next succeeding nights, but no
one came for his bread and milk. The fourth night
Hanna was consumed with a desire to sleep, and. so he
sought his bed. The following morning he rushed to
the cupboard â the pound of cheese, the butter and the
pickles were gone. Hanna is now editing a dictionary
on profanity. He will be present at the trial of the fair
Lisa, the deft and apt lady of the Mission.
NEW STRENGTH
FOR THE NAVY
One Hundred Appren
tices Wanted.
LIEUT. BURNETT'S ORDERS
POSSIBLE PREPARATION* FOR
THE FUTURE.
Renewed Activity at the Naval Ren
dezvous and the Coaling of the
Bennington Considered
Significant,
After a per* 0 * o* several months of In
activity the naval rendezvous at 10 Cali
fornia street had orders yesterday from
the Bureau of Navigation at Washington
to recruit another 100 naval apprentice
boys. whoÃe service will begin on the
Pensacola at Mare Island. Lieutenant J.
C. Burnett, in charge of the recruiting of
fice, will be ready to-day to receive ap
plications for this enlistment.
This order for increasing the naval ap
prentices and the fact that the gunboat
Bennington is now coaling at this port for
the probable destniation of Guatemala are
looked upon in naval circles as an Indi
cation that the United States Govern
ment Is keeping an eye on ports and
islands under control of or dominated by
Spain; and that instead of decreasing the
navy now at the closing of the war, the
intention is to keep It up to its present
standard of excellence with an increase
in the number of men and commissioned
ships. '
The last enlistment of apprentices pro
vided an excellent class of boys, and
Lieutenant Burnett hopes to secure an
equally good class in the present enlist
ment They must be of an age between
the fourteenth and seventeenth birthdays
and be subjected to a severe physlcial
examination that cannot be passed by
cigarette smokers.

SHE CLUNG TO THE CAN.
Mrs. Golding Would Not Give Up Her
Growler Even in the Jaws
of Death.
If a congealed fact proves anything, the "growler"
habit is Invincible, even in the face of death. This
proposition was made evident yesterday during the
wild touch-and-go experiences of Mrs. Golding, who
resides on Jessie street She has tripped through the
lights and shadows of seventy years, and is still as
lively as the proverbial cricket. Age nor indulgence
have cloyed her taste for the poor man's beverageâ
steam beerâ and be It said in sorrow that the ancient
matron is a confirmed "rusher of the growler."
She started from her humble home yesterday, carry
ing her faithful well-worn tin can and a. nickel with
which to purchased potion of the amber fluid. It was
a case of presence of body and absence of mind. Her
whole thought was centered on the diÃtant corner,
where the cool, fat barrels tempt the patron's thirst,
'the rush and roar, the clang and clatter of a great
city she heeded not,
""She was thirsty. Her crying need was beer.
The deadly cable car was hurrying to the ferry, and
as usual, it did not get out of the way. Neither did
Mrs. Golding. When they picked the poor, shabbily
garbed old body up the drawn face was sorely bruised,
and she was insensible to the pangs of pain or appetite.
Kind hands carried her into the car, while the bony fin
gers clung to the can with a death grip. At the ferry
the sufferer was transferred to an electric car on East
street, and still she clung to the can.
At the corner of Mission and EaÃt streets she was'
tenderly lifted Into a stretcher to be carried to the Re
ceiving Hospital. And the bony hand still clung to the
can. .On the operating table, when the good doctor
dressed the wounds, it was the same old story; the
venerable victim still clung to the can. Then she re
gained'consciousness and discovered that the priceless
nickel â the key to the beer faucet â was lost â and her
grief was deplorable. The battered face, the aching
bones, the torn remnants of a dress,^ were as naught,
for the visions of beer and cheer had vanished in thÂ«
busy street, to make glad thÂ« heart of the hapless
Bweeper.
But, 10, the sweet angel of charity was abroad In the
land in the person of Dr. Hill, who pressed a shining
dime into the trembling palm. The old lady content
edly tottered beerward. She still clung to the can, and
when she got home the can was full.

WANTS TB.E WILL REVOKED.
Charles Rathgeb Alleges That His
Brother Was Unduly Influenced.
OAKLAND, Aug. 17.â Charles Rathgeb
has petitioned the Superior Court for the
revocation of the probate of the will of
his brother, John Rathgeb. Petitioner
has not heretofore appeared in matters
concerning his brother's will, which was
admitted to probate August 30 last year.
A revocation is asked on the grounds that
said will was not the last will; that it was
not executed by John Rathgeb: that the
latter was not of sound mind and was in
competent to make a will; that at the date
of the pretended execution of said will
Albert Guttlnger was tho sole and confi
dential agent of the testator and had
freat influence and control over him, and
y undue influence procured and induced
said John Rathgeb to make the pretended 1
will and to disinherit petitioner; also that
enemies of petitioner unduly Influenced
testator to disinherit him.
The devisees in the will the revocation
of which is sought are {Catherine Guttin
ger. Maria Hoffman, Hans. Albert and
Caroline Guttlnger, all of Zurich; Albert
Guttinger and Frank Bernasconl of San
Andreas, Emil Rathgeb of Chicago. Ber
tha C, Julius, Henry. Emll. Emily. Ber
tha and Cella Bosschart of Clinton, lowa.
The heirs at law residing in this State are
Emma T. Rathgeb. the widow, of Oak
land; Charles Rathgeb. Napa; Rudolph
and John B. Rathgeb, San Andreas. The
executors of the will are Frank Bernas
coni. Jules Agostini and ATT>ert Guttinger.
John Rathgeb died in Alameda July â¢;,
1897, and left a valuable estate.

THE ARIZONA
IS TO SAIL
ON SATURDAY
Scandia Will Follow
Next Week.
BOTH ARE TO CARRY TROOPS
THE LATTER TO BE USED Afci
A HOSPITAL SHIP.
In Case Merritt Wants More Troops
the Seventh California Will Be
the- Next Regiment
to Go.
The cloud of disappointment that has
been hanging over Camp Morritt was dis
pelled yeaterday morning by the an
nouncement that General Merriam had
received a telegram from tne Secretary
of War authorizing him at once to. send
to Manila the transport Arizona with the
troops originally designated to go on that
steamer- This authorization is the result
of General Merriam'.s suggestion that the
incomplete regiments now at Manila
should be filled up with the detachments
and ncruits belonging to them and yet
remaining here. The troops that were
announced to go on the Arizona were one
battalion of the Eighteenth infantry, one
battalion of the Twenty-third Infantry
and recruits Cor the Pennsylvania, Colo
rado, Oregon and Nebraska regiments,
and nurses and hospital corps, with quite

THE AUSTBALIA RETURNS.
Another Transport Arrives From Ma
nila With Seven Sick Soldier
Boys Aboard.
The United States transport Australia
arrived from Manila, via Nagasaki, yes
terday. She brought back seven soldiers,
â some of wh<#n went down on her to the
Philippines. 'Private. Robert A. Allen of
Company B; Private W. J. Shelly. Corn-
Volunteers, lost his reason on the way
down and had to be sent home. His rela
tives live in Alameda. Of the Second Ore
gon regiment. Private William Warm.
Company B; Private W. J. Shell, Com
pany G; Private George K. Brown, Com
pany G; Private Walter H. Dye, Sergeant
Charles A. Bort of Company X, all came
back on the teick list, while Private L. H.
-Joy of the Fourteenth Infantry came
back because his term of service had ex-
Thomas A. Hallett, third officer of the
Australia, was a very sick man when the
vessel reached port. He is suffering from
drnpsy and may never be able to go to
Bea again. Hallett is an old whaler, and
in turn commanded the Herman, Rein
deer Andr-w Hicks, Jeannette and Bel
vedere. Many a time, when whaling in the
southern Beas, did he call at the island of
Guam, and he knows the entrance to it
as well as he dots the Golden Gate. When
the fleet was a day's sail from the Island
n boat was sont f>om the Charleston to
bring Captain Hallett aboard the cruiser.
He was appointed pilot and next day
took the warship into port. He then pi
loted the City of Peking. Australia and
City of Sydney in, in turn.
The boys on the Australia brought up a
great many curios, but the most curious
is a small "black pig. It was procured at
Guam and soon became the pet of the
ship. >
THOMAS TO BE PAID.
Tennesseeans Will Promptly
Repair the Property Which
They Damaged.
The developments In the case of the
Tennessee regiment Beem to have taken
a turn for the better, and such a turn as
clearly shows that Colonel Smith and
his officers not only deeply regret the
unfortunate affair, but are determined
to make all amends in their power with
out waiting for the tardy wheels of mili
tary justice. Yesterday Colonel Smith
sent Lieutenant Fowler into the city for
a contractor, who will at the expense of
the regiment put Thomas' house in just
as good and even better condition than
it was before it became the object of de
struction at the hands of the soldiers.
"This, of course, will do away with the
board of survey, which would have had to
have been appointed by General Miller
to assess the damages," said Colonel
Smith "This does not mean that the of
fenders will not be just as severely dealt
with. Lieutenant Fowler is conducting
the investigation as rapidly as possible,
but there are several facts in the case
that it will require some, little diplomacy
and time to secure, and as soon as these
have been obtained the men concerned
will be punished. Privates X>avls. Ander
son, Clarke and Scruggs are now in the
guardhouse."
TO ASSIST THE SEVENTH.
A Request for These Volunteers
to Be Sent to Manila.
The Chamber of Commerce is using its
best efforts to assist the Seventh Regi
ment of California Volunteers to get to
the front, the following dispatch being
Bent yesterday by President Hugh Craig
of the Chamber to President McKinley.
Later in the day an answer was received
from Secretary Algcr:
To the President, Washington. D. C. : The
Seventh Regiment of <'altfornia was the first
to leave their homes and muster in. Have been
over three months at Camp Merritt. Have' been
repeatedly promised transportation to Manila,
and. now. fear hein^' turned down. California
claims the Philippine expedition as Its own.
It is one of our colonies Jtke Hawaii. We have
hald -up the hands of the administration and
held n"thinÂ£ back from your good self, and
now ask as a special favor to the Chamber of
Commerce of San Francisco that the Seventh
Regiment of California be detailed for Manila
. by the Arizona, now ready to sell at a mo
ment's notice. HUGH CHAIG.

War Incidents.
The concert to be given by the Fourth
Cavalry Band to-morrow afternoon, un- .
der the direction of Chief Musician Lit- i
tleton, promises to be a very entertain- [
ing affair. The organization has been
augmented by musicians from the dif
ferent bands now at the post, and will
number over sixty instruments.
Private John Reed, Company I, Fifty
first lowa Regiment, died at the Divis
ion Hospital late on Tuesday night of
pneumonia, following measles.
Major Ruhlen goes to Honolulu on the
Arizona to take the position of post and
depot quartermaster, with the rank of
lieutenant colonel.
The bids for the water supply at Camp
Baker will be opened on the 25th. The
contract will provide for the construction
of a tank, a pump-house and deep-bore
well, and the faying of 5000 feet of pipe.
Lieutenant John O'Shea, recently ap
pointed first lieutenant of Troop L.
Fourth United States Cavalry, but who
has been temporarily assigned to Troop
B, reported to General Miller for duty
yesterday. Lieutenant O'Shea has been
In the service for fourteen years, and
rose from the ranks. He has seen serv
ice in all branches. of the army. His last

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, AUGUST 18, 1898.
UKIAH PASTOR
IN A SCANDAL
Flutter in the Baptist
Congregation.
GIDDY LAMB OF THE FLOCK
A CLANDESTINE MEETING AND
ITS RESULTS.
Minister Tried and Acquitted, but
Resigns â Now Young and Old
Members of the CJhurch.
Are at War.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
URIAH, Aug. 17.â Trouble has come to
the First Baptist Church of this city, the
largest and most influential organization
of the kind in the county. The cause is
alleged to be the unusual interest taken
in a lady member of the church by Rev.
B. J. Farmer, the pastor. As a result, a
resignation and a church trial, both evi
dently unsatisfactory, have occurred,
leaving the older element of the congrega
tion at war with the younger.
Rev. Mr. Farmer was formerly a sur
veyor in Santa Rosa, and has wealthy
relatives there. He came to Ukiah about
a year ago, after successful pastorates in
Humboldt County and Potter Valley, in
thia county. In order that her spiritual
needs might not be neglected, it is assert
ed, Mrs. E. Smith, a member of the Potter
Valley fold, followed shortly afterward.
For some time she has conducted the
hotel formerly known as the Donahue
No SS t c "long ago the tongue of gossip be
gan to wag on account of the interest
manifested in Mrs. Smith by her pastor.
Two weeks ago a culmination was
reached by Mrs. William Southard, a
! Baptist church member, informing one or
! the deacons that Mr* Smith was known
i to have entered a room lh her hotel in
company with Rev. Mr. Farmer and to
have remained there behind locked doors
fur three hours. This statement created
general consternation, and led to the ap
pointment of a court of Inquiry, consist
ing of Deacons M. W. Howard, Edward
Cox and B. Hutchinson.
A strenuous effort was made to keep
the scandal from becoming the property
of the worldly with excellent success A
secret trial was held last Saturday after
noon, and Mrs. Smith was called to tes
tify. She admitted the accusation of Mrs
Southard, but said her husband had asked
the preacher to see her on account of
trouble in the family. Thr. husband jms
present and refused to testify either way
Preacher Farmer asserted his relations
: with Mrs. Smith to be purely platonlc.
1 and for lack of evidence the charges were
d T^n Se the minister was asked to resign,
and did so promptly out the younger ele
ment the late accretions, refused to ac
cept "the resignation. Jhe youthful con
tingent even went so far in adding fuel
to the fires of bitterness as to try to ha\e
Mrs Southard expelled without a hearing
as a source of trouble, notwithstanding
the fact that the allegations made by her
W What r the d outcome will be is not known
A peculiar phase of the situation is that
| onlva little more than two years ago the
church was enveloped in a cloud of scan
da" over the alleged misdoing of Rev. Ar
i nold IJndsav, p former pastor, all the
older members standing squarely for him
against the younper contingent.

SAN JOSE'S HIGH
SCHOOL WRECKED
Politics Has Virtually
Destroyed It.
EFFECT OF SMITH'S REMOVAL
PUPILS WILL FOLLOW HIM TO
SANTA CLARA.
Many Residents of the Garden City
Contemplate Moving in Order
to Be With Their
Children.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN JOSE, Aug.l 7.â lnterest Is increas
ing in the High School troubles as the
time for opening the school approaches.
August 29 promises to find our new High
School practically empty. Of over 300 pu
pils in attendance last year, not over 100
will put in an appearance.
At the Santa Clara High School, to
which Professor Smith has been elected
principal, over 150 pupils will attend who
have been attending the school here. The
Santa Clara School Trustees have made
all preparations to receive the increase.
School rooms have been fitted with the
best school furniture and the gymnasium
has been turned into a laboratory, which
Is as well appointed as any on the coast.
Not only has the city Board of Educa
tion wrecked our High School but their
acts have been the means of driving
thousands of dollars away from San Jose.
The dismissal of Professor Smith has Ir
reparably injured the High School. Prin
cipal A. E. Shumate, Cornelia B. Farley,
Mrs. Pillot and Mrs. Sawyer are also se
verely criticized by the people, for it was
the Jealousy of these persons thart was
the prime cause of Smith's removal.
Santa Clara made a great popular move
when It elected Smith principal of its
High School. The town is experiencing a
boom through its school. Families who
lived in this city so as to educate their
children. ln our schools have moved there,
and Santa Clara real estate agents are In
daily receipt of letters asking for empty
houses.
The tuition at the Santa Clara School
has been placed at $1S per year for one
or $30 for two members of the same
family. A fund is being raised In this
city to pay the tuition of the students
who will a"ttend. Charges of immorality
in the faculty of the local school are
openly made, and the people of the city
prefer to have their children go else
where. The committee having the fund
in hand is meeting with great success.
Last year San Jose received $2607 as
tuition from students living outside the
city, but this year Santa Clara will re
ceive this money. All pay students who
attended here will surely go to Santa
Clara.
Professor Smith was the best teacher
ever employed in the San Jose High
School. This is best evidenced from the
standing his pupila attained at the uni
versities. To satisfy the dictates of
"Boss" Rea the city Board of Education
dismissed him. The pupils have solemn
ly declared they will not attend the High
School next year. Their parents and
the better element of the city are back
of them, and the new High School will
present a deserted appearance at the be
ginning of next term.
Professor Smith has received all kinds
of propositions from our citizens, who
are almost a unit in denouncing Rea and
the school board for the parts they play
ed in the matter. A dozen or so of the
most prominent and influential people
in the city wanted Professor Smith to
establish a university school here. They
offered him all the capital needed. The
plans were to lease the magnificent prop
erty formerly belonging to Mrs. Murphy-
Colombet, on Fifth and William streets.
The place would make an ideal school.

Death of Thomas M. Jewett.
Thomas M. Jewett, who up to a few
months ago had been for thirty-four
years chief clerk in the office of the
United States District Attorney, died last
night in the State Insane Asylum at Ag
news.
The deceased was smitten with pa
ralysis about a year ago while on a
vacation In Chihuahua, Mexico, and was
never able afterward to discharge the
duties of his office. He had lost the power
of speech, and though able to move about
seemed to be unable to care for himself.
Recently he became blind and morose,
and was quietly committed to the insane
asylum. The deceased leaves a widow
and four children.
Grand Larceny Alleged.
Minnie Buchlar, 231 Kearny street,
swore to a complaint in Judge Oonlan's
court yesterday for the arrest of Edward
Wyman on the charge of grand larceny.
She met Wyman a few days ago, and,
she alleges, he stole a diamond pin and a
diamond ring from her.
The Railroad Case.
Virgil Bogue testified yesterday In the
case of the Sotithern Pacific Company
vs. the Railroad Commissioners: as to the
value of the property of the railroad com
pany He said that the valuation made
by the company was correct.

DEATH WAS AT THE HELM OF AN EXCURSION YACHT.
Mrs. Paxton Drowned
in the Waters of
the Bay.
LAUNCH WAS SUNK '
IN A COLLISION
ALL ON BOARD WERE SAVED
BUT HER.
Her Dress Caught in the Machinery
and She Is Supposed to Have
. " Gone Down With the
Boat.
The new launch of the United States
transport Scandia while out on a pleas
ure trip yesterday morning was run
. down and sunk by the United States
quartermaster's steamer General Me
. Dowell. Thero were ten people aboard
the launch and of these Mrs. Charles
E. Paxton, wife of the well-known min
ing man and stock broker, was
â¢ , drowned.
â¢ The launch "was towed down from
Ware Island last Monday by the navy
yard tug Unadilla. Monday afternoon
she was taken out for her trial trip and
a number of ladies who saw the hand
some little boat from the deck of the
Scandia expressed a wish to go on a
trip around the bay In her. Captain
Nat T. Messer, who represents the
quartermaster on the troopship, at once
agreed to take them out and yesterday
morning was fixed upon for the excur
sion.
When the launch started out on the
trip there were aboard: Mrs. Charles E.
Paxton, Mrs. Douglas S. Cone, Mrs.
Margaret A. Peterson, N. Thayer Mes
ser Jr., Captain Frank W. Hart of the
Scandia, Third Officer H. A. Thompson,
Quartermasters O. Jones and S. Lu
brano, Assistant Engineer Don Hillman
and Fireman John Daly, all of the
troopship.
The General McDowell had been on a
special trip to Alcatraz to pump water
Into the reservoir and was in charge of
First Mate Peter Grimm, as Captain
John J. Stofen was on leave. Only one
or two soldiers from the island were
aboard besides the crew, and the vessel
was under a full head of steam making
for her berth at Clay-street wharf.
The McDowell is a notoriously hard
vessel to handle, particularly while
. backing, and in consequence it has al
ways been the custom to run her along
the wharves so as to make a short turn
Into her slip. Consequently the launch
was sunk midway between the Jackson
and Pacific street wharves, and not fifty
yards from the end of either.
The stories of the disaster agree in
the main and it will rest with the Unit
ed States Inspectors of Hulls and Boil
ers to fix the blame. As the launch
ptnrtftd out of the slip Third Officer
Thompson, as is customary, blew a long
whistle to notify vessels on the outside
that he was coming. Mate Grimm, on
the McDowell, heard the signal and
replied with one whistle, signifying that
he was about to make a landing and

Captain Hart states most positively

Mrs. Paxton was a Miss Florence
Lewis and about 30 years of age. Her
mother, Mrs. Dr. Emily A. Lewis, lives
at 2115 Webster street. Another sis
ter who was in the accident is the wife

Her brother, Frank Lewis, is a clerk

was an intimate friend of the late Mrs.
James L. Flood and it seems a strange
coincidence that her death should fol
low so quickly upon that of her friend.
The Paxtons and the Messers were
also great friends and it was this
friendship which caused the former to
leave their home at 2211 Washington
street and take rooms at the Hotel
Bella Vista, where the Messers reside.
Mrs. Paxton was a beautiful woman
and had a host of friends in this city.
Soon after the accident a number of
boats began grappling for the sunken
launch. She was soon located and a
skiff was anchored over the spot. At
6:50 p. m., slack water, a diver was sent
down and in ten minutes he had a rope
fastened around the sunken launch.
The line was then made fast to the
winch on the scow from which the
diver went down and the small boat
was soon off the mud.
Both scow and submerged launch
were then taken in tow and placed
alongside the Soandia, The steamer's
powerful steam winches were soon put
to work and the launch hoisted aboard.
There was no trace of the body of
Mrs. Paxton in the wreck and the gen
eral impression ia that it was washed
out by the tide and carried away. Just
off Pacific and Jackson street wharves
and in fact all along the front the cur
rent runs like a mill race and the
chances are that the body will be car
ried away down to the Presidio and
maybe out to Bea.
