POLICE OFFICER THOMAS LANGFORD.
HE FEARED
THE POLICE
TRIBUNAL
Suicide of Officer Tom
Langford.
SHOT HIMSELF IN THE HEAD
THOUGHT HE WAS TO BE DIS
GRACED.
Tragic Ending of One of the Most
Popular Members of the
Police Depart
ment.
Financial trouble and the fear that
he would be brought before the Police
Commissioners impelled Officer Tom
Langford to commit suicide last even
ing by shooting himself in the head.
The deed was done at his residence,
2927 Octavia street.
Langford returned home shortly after
6 o'clock and was met by his 8-year-old
daughter. Picking her up in his arms
he passionately kissed her and then en
tered the house. His wife was engaged
in preparing the evening meal, and
after addressing her he sat down In the
dining-room. A few minutes later a
shot rang out. Mrs. Langford rushed
into the dining-room, and seated in a
chair sat her husband, with a bullet
hole In his head. On the floor, within
a few feet of the chair, was the revolver
with which the deed was done.
Police Officer Carroll was passing at
the time, and, rushing into the house,
he found Mrs. Langford standing over
her husband, vainly attempting to
stanch the flow of blood. She was cry-
Ing pitifully, frequently discontinuing
herefforts to stop the flow of blood to
kiss the colorless face of her dying hus
band. Carroll, realizing that Langford
was mortally wounded, at once sum
moned the patrol wagon.
The dying man was placed in It and
hurriedly driven to the Receiving Hos
pital. Drs. Wymore and Graves, after
an examination, realized that he was
beyond all human aid. They washed
the blood from his face and had him
carried into one of the wards, where he
was placed on a cot. A short time after
ward his wife, escorted by Sergeant of
Police Bethel, visited the hospital.
With a wild shriek Mrs. Langford fell
on her husband and begged him to
speak to her.
"I know he did not commit suicide,"
she moaned, "he had no reason to kill
himself."
When asked about the tragic occur
ence Mrs. Langford declared that the
pistol must have been accidentally dis
charged.
"We have always lived happily to
gether," she remarked. "Tom was a
good and true husband, and as far as I
know he did not have an enemy. Yes
terday he told me he had arrested a
man named Cunningham for vagrancy,
and that he threatened to bring him
before the Police Commissioners. The
threat seemed to worry him, but my
cheering words had a good effect on j
him.
It is true that he has had some finan
cial trouble, but as they were of such
a trivial nature I thoueht he had for
gotten them. Only a short time ago,
while he was examining his revolver,
it was discharged, the bullet lodging in
the wall. I am sure that after sitting
down he took out the revolver to ex- j
amine it, and while doing so it was ac- I
cidentally discharged the bullet taking I
effect in the head.
An examination of the wound by the j
surgeons at the hospital showed that I
the bullet had entered the right side \
of the head, passed through the brain j
and lodged over the left eve.
Some years ago the father of Lang- (
ford shot himself in a similar manner. I
He was a sergeant of police, and, be- ,
coming despondent, he decided to end !
his existence.
On the table in the room In which the
despondent officer shot himself was a
letter, which he evidently had been j
reading. It was written in a pencil and
read as follows:
Dear Lou: I don't know that lam
helping you out any, but will ask you if
yÂ»B were around Pine and Montgomery
streets the day you lost your glasses. I !
Just heard a young lady say that she i
found a pair of pold glasses on Pine and j
Montgomery streets about three weeks!
ago. She sold them to a man whose name j
I do not know. I looked in the papers to '
see if you had advertised for them, but i
dl iL n ?l see * U - How dld >'ou Set along i
with the dress? May has written to
James. We got a letter from him on
Monday. Ma answered it. We are wait
ing to hear from you. In haste
MAMIE.
Who "Lou" or "Mamie" is the rela- i
tlves of Langford are at a loss to know. '
Their only explanation is that he !
picked up the letter while patrolling
hiÃ beat and started to read It after he
had reached his home.
The unfortunate man passed away
shortly after 9 o'clock, surrounded by
his wife and several fellow policemen.
Langford had been connected with the
police force for about six years and was j
held in high esteem by his superior of- J
fleers. Yesterday afternoon he called 1
on Chief Lees and asked him if it were <:
true that Cunningham intended to pre- j ,
fer charges against him. The chief told â¢

were preferred. It is supposed that i

BEATEN BY HER DAUGHTERS.
Pitiful Story Told by Mrs. Nellie
Berry of Chattanooga Street.
Mrs. Nellie Berry, 118 Chattanoopa
street, called at the Receiving Hospital
yesterday morning: and had two lacerated
wounds In her scalp stitched and dressed
by Dr. Rlnne. Amid sobs she told a piti
ful story.
She said that about S o'clock she awak
ened her two daughters, Mollle
22 years of age. and Nellie. 19. Mollle
was angry, and rushing at her struck her
on the head with the heel of her shoe,
inflicting the wounds. She begged her
daughters to give her 10 cents so that she
could take a car to the hospital to get
her wounds attended to, but they refuse,d
and- she was compelled to walk all the
way.
Her husband deserted her on June 13,
after drawing $1000 out of the Hibernia
Bank, which they had managed to save, !
and went to Honolulu. He was a brick- i
layer. Since then her two daughters had
been treating her cruelly and doing cv- !
erything to drive her out of the house
Bo that they could have entire pos
session. She had been compelled to live
and sleep in the kitchen, while her daugh
ters occupied the rest of the house and
lived sumptuously.
On July 4 they beat her so badly that
she had to go to the City and County !
Hospital for treatment. They had also '
endeavorod to have her arrested for in- j
sanity, but did not succeed. One of her
daughters was employed in a millinery |
store on Market street, near Mason, and I
the other kept houst.
CHINESE LOTTERY DEAL.
The Tai Loy Company Refuses to
Honor a Ticket That Won a
Big Prize.
The ways of the Chinese lottery men are '
peculiar and may be exposed in the Police j
Court. The Tal I,oy Company is the of
fending organization, through its mana
ger. Buck Gui, agent for "Big Jim."
Moses Silverstein, who has an office in
the Mills building, purchased a ticket i
from a Chinese lottery dealer a few nights j
ago. The agent was arrested the same i
night by the Chinatown squad, and when '
searched fifteen tickets were found in his
possession, one of them bearing Silver
stein's name on the back. A few hours
later the Chinese was bailed out and the
Chinese who deposited the bail on look
!"F! "F at the tickets said that the one with :
*HiÂ£ ers * â¦I? 5 n . ame . had drawn a prize of j
$1200 at that night's drawing.
When the ticket was presented the next '
day at the office of the Tai Loy Com- I
pany payment was refused. Buck Gui as
serting that the ticket had not drawn a
prize and thjtt the police and- the agent
had combined to swindle the company by
marking tho ticket after the results of
the drawing had become known
The action of the company is the talk
of Chinatown, and will have the effoct of
doing considerable injury to the Chinese
lottery business, especially among white
people. Silverstein has placed the matter i
In the hands of Attorney Archie Camp
bell.
